{"code":1,"isDone":false,"toInsertArticleList":[{"createdTime":"2021-05-19 22:56:01","updatedTime":"2021-05-19 22:56:01","title":"How WallStreetBets “hype” spreads among investors like a virus","link":"https://bigthink.com/mind-brain/wallstreetbets","description":"<img src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjM4NTA5NC9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYzMDAwNzI4NX0.LsqUySoIjBxXnIrVhrjCd1U5kHIENE9bOnMM1jUtupk/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C222%2C0%2C318&height=700\"/><br/><br/><ul> <li>The study found evidence that \"hype\" over assets is psychologically contagious among investors in online communities.</li><li>This hype is self-perpetuating: A small group of investors hypes an asset, bringing in new investors, until growth becomes unsteady and a price crash ensues.</li><li>The researchers suggested that these new kinds of self-organized, social media-driven investment behaviors are unlikely to disappear anytime soon.</li></ul><hr/><p>Social media has reshaped human behavior in ways we're only starting to understand. The proliferation of online communities has helped spawn novel strategies for promoting political causes, conducting business, finding sex and love, and transforming culture.</p><p>Could online communities also transform behavior in the financial world?</p><p>That's one of the key questions explored in a new <a href=\"https://arxiv.org/pdf/2104.01847.pdf\" target=\"_blank\">study</a> published on the preprint server arXiv. Titled \"Reddit's self-organised bull runs: Social contagion and asset prices,\" the study used discussion data from the subreddit <a href=\"https://www.reddit.com/r/wallstreetbets/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">WallStreetBets</a> to analyze relationships between the price of stocks and \"hype\" among online retail investors.</p><p>Hype is nothing new in the investing world. But the researchers noted that there seems to be something novel about the <a href=\"https://www.investopedia.com/terms/s/shortsqueeze.asp\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">short squeeze</a> of GameStop's stock in January, when the price of the stock rose tenfold, thanks largely to self-organized retail investors from WallStreetBets.</p><p>\"As academics and regulators alike grapple with the implications, many wonder whether large-scale coordination among retail investors is the new 'modus operandi,' or a one-off fluke,\" the researchers wrote. \"We argue that this is a new manifestation of a well-established global phenomenon.\"</p><p>To better understand how online hype is associated with stock prices, the researchers focused on two social components of hype: contagion and consensus. Contagion refers to investors spreading interest in an asset among each other, while consensus refers to their ability to agree on whether to buy or sell an asset.</p><p>The analysis found empirical evidence that both contagion and consensus emerge in online communities like WallStreetBets. In other words, investors spread sentiments about future stock performance to other investors, and then they cohere around investment strategies. </p><h2>Popularity over fundamentals</h2><p>The findings suggest that an asset's popularity, not its fundamentals, is paramount to many investors.</p><p>\"Our results consistently show that investors become interested in discussing an asset, not because of fundamentals, but because other users discuss it,\" the researchers wrote. \"Subsequently, this paper tests whether an individual's sentiment about future asset performance [is] affected by those of others. We find that this is the case: people look to their peers to form an opinion about an asset's potential.\"</p><p>To find evidence for social contagion among online investors, the researchers compiled a large dataset of posts and comments submitted to WallStreetBets. The goal was to analyze whether investors' past comments or posts about a given stock, such as Tesla, had a predictable effect on future discussions of that asset within WallStreetBets. </p><p>After conducting a regression analysis, the results suggest that hype is socially contagious and cyclical. The cycle usually plays out like this: A small group of investors hypes an asset. This attracts a larger group of investors who join the discussions.</p><p><br/></p><p class=\"shortcode-media shortcode-media-rebelmouse-image\">\n        <img class=\"rm-shortcode\" data-rm-shortcode-id=\"0345eeaab6a3dbdd512388b1fef3e01a\" data-rm-shortcode-name=\"rebelmouse-image\" id=\"93cf8\" loading=\"lazy\" src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjM4NTA4OC9vcmlnaW4ucG5nIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYzNzUyNzQzMn0.xY8sUsIJNmNRRshrkUBPd4BicFzWCOAYLyK7plDZYk8/img.png?width=980\"/>\n        \n        \n        <small class=\"image-media media-photo-credit\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Credit...\"><a href=\"https://arxiv.org/pdf/2104.01847.pdf\" target=\"_blank\">Semenova & Winkler</a></small></p><p>But eventually, too many investors have joined the discussion, and fewer new investors are buying into the hype. As investors lose interest, they spend less time discussing (or \"spreading\") the asset on the forum, and they turn to new opportunities. The process is similar to a virus: As enough people become infected, they reach herd immunity, and the virus (hype) dies out.<br/></p><p>So, does this process affect the stock price, and if so, how? The researchers said it was difficult to establish causality between hype and actual market activity. After all, they didn't have access to the trading records of subscribers to WallStreetBets. </p><p>But their model did show that activity on WallStreetBets was able to explain \"significant variance\" in trading volumes for the most-discussed assets on the forum. This suggests that when social contagion is strong for a given asset, consensus is strong too. </p><p>On the stock chart, consensus may start off bullish (or positively): As hype spreads, there's a slow, steady run-up in price. But the growth eventually becomes unstable and is followed by a crash and a period of volatility. </p><p>\"The price crash stems from panic selling, as investors turn nervous in the face of volatility,\" the researchers wrote.</p><h2>Bad news spreads faster than good news</h2><p>Interestingly, the analysis found that bearish (or negative) sentiments were significantly more contagious on WallStreetBets. </p><p style=\"margin-left: 20px;\">\"The data demonstrates that authors who previously commented on a bearish post are 47.7% more likely to express bearish over neutral sentiments, and 18.1% less likely to express bullish sentiments over neutral sentiments. Similarly, but less markedly, authors who previously commented on at least one bullish submission are 9.4% more likely to write a bullish submission, yet 11.3% less likely to write a bearish one.\"</p><p>The researchers said that the changing investing climate and widely available online data offers \"promising opportunities for future research.\"</p><p>\"As social media galvanizes a larger pool of retail investors with the potential for exciting stock market gambles, it is crucial to understand how social dynamics can impact asset prices,\" the researchers wrote. \"With the first publicly acclaimed victory of Main Street over Wall Street, in the form of the GameStop short squeeze, it is unlikely that socially-driven asset volatility will simply disappear.\"</p><div></div><ul></ul><div></div><img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/toL0_xTrszg\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" 22:56:01","updatedTime":"2021-05-19 22:56:01","title":"How to fool a shark using magnets","link":"https://bigthink.com/surprising-science/shark-magnetic","description":"<img src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjM3ODE3OS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYzNDczOTE3OX0.fwHyqvncgcGBEp-aM-J_-1pFeHPqrTotRSRJxqa16Qo/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C0%2C0%2C0&height=700\"/><br/><br/><ul><li>It's long been suspected that sharks navigate the oceans using Earth's magnetic field.</li><li><span></span>Sharks are, however, difficult to experiment with.</li><li>Using magnetism, marine biologists figured out a clever way to fool sharks into thinking they're somewhere that they're not.</li></ul><hr/><p>For some time, scientists have suspected that sharks belong among the growing number of animals known to navigate using Earth's magnetic field. Testing anything with a shark, though, requires some care. </p><p>\"The reason this question has been withstanding for 50 years is because sharks are difficult to study,\" <a href=\"https://www.eurekalert.org/pub_releases/2021-05/cp-sue042921.php\" target=\"_blank\">says</a> <a href=\"https://saveourseas.com/project-leader/bryan-keller-2/\" target=\"_blank\">Bryan Keller</a> of the Save Our Seas Foundation. \"To be honest, I am surprised it worked.\"</p><p>The key was selecting the right candidate. Keller and his colleagues chose the bonnethead shark, <em>Sphyrna tiburo</em>, a small critter that summers at Turkey Point Shoal off the coast of the Florida State University Coastal and Marine Laboratory with which Keller is affiliated.</p><p>Bonnetheads elsewhere have been known to complete 620-mile roundtrip migrations. As the lab's Dean Grubbs <a href=\"https://news.fsu.edu/news/2021/05/06/navigational-tools-sharks-use-earths-magnetic-fields-to-find-their-way-home/\" target=\"_blank\">puts</a> it, \"That's not bad for a shark that is only two to three feet long. The question is how do they find their way back to that same estuary year after year.\" There's a report of a <a href=\"https://www.whitesharktrust.org/migration.html\" target=\"_blank\">great white shark</a> migrating between two locations, one in South Africa and another in Australia, year after year.</p><p>The research is published in <a href=\"https://www.cell.com/current-biology/fulltext/S0960-9822(21)00476-0?utm_source=EA\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"><em>Current Biology</em></a>.</p><h2>Shark tank</h2><p class=\"shortcode-media shortcode-media-rebelmouse-image\">\n        <img class=\"rm-shortcode\" data-rm-shortcode-id=\"2e41b5dbaca1b3918ebc8a5b253eeff7\" data-rm-shortcode-name=\"rebelmouse-image\" id=\"a2b6c\" loading=\"lazy\" src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjM3ODE4OC9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYzMTQzOTUzN30.xYEsHvByJ6sWAg4V60GRM8Hm-JReP2lMvYjQXvqm5BM/img.jpg?width=980\"/>\n        \n        \n        <small class=\"image-media media-caption\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Caption...\"><a href=\"https://www.cell.com/current-biology/pdfExtended/S0960-9822(21)00476-0\">These are the three places the bonnethead sharks were magnetically fooled into thinking they were located.</a></small><small class=\"image-media media-photo-credit\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Credit...\"><a href=\"https://www.cell.com/current-biology/pdfExtended/S0960-9822(21)00476-0\">Credit: Keller et al. / Current Biology</a></small></p><p>Keller and his team rounded up 20 local juvenile bonnetheads and transported them into a holding tank at the marine lab. For the tests, the researchers simulated three real-world magnetic fields. As the various magnetic fields were activated, the sharks' movements were captured by GoPro cameras and their average swimming orientations calculated by software.<br/></p><p>The first simulation, serving as a control, mimicked the magnetic field of the nearby shoal from which the sharks had been captured. When this field was activated, the sharks essentially acted like they were \"home,\" just swimming around as they do.</p><p>A second field was the magnetic equivalent of a location 600 kilometers south of the lab within the Gulf of Mexico. When this field was activated, the sharks, apparently mistaking themselves for being far south in the Gulf, began swimming northward toward the shoal.</p><p>The opposite occurred with a field standing in for a location in continental North America 600 km north of their home shoal — the sharks began swimming southward.</p><h2>Suspicion confirmed</h2><p>\"For 50 years,\" says Keller, \"scientists have hypothesized that sharks use the magnetic field as a navigational aid. This theory has been so popular because sharks, skates, and rays have been shown to be very sensitive to magnetic fields. They have also been trained to react to unique geomagnetic signatures, so we know they are capable of detecting and reacting to variation in the magnetic field.\"</p><p>His team's experiments confirm what's long been suspected, Keller says: \"Sharks use map-like information from the geomagnetic field as a navigational aid. This ability is useful for navigation and possibly maintaining population structure.\"</p><img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/4mo4Yp_5dCA\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\"/>","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Wed, 12 May 2021 13:00:01 22:56:01","updatedTime":"2021-05-19 22:56:01","title":"Intolerance of uncertainty drives liberals and conservatives to polarizing partisanship","link":"https://bigthink.com/politics-current-affairs/political-polarization-2653027840","description":"<img src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjQwOTc1Ni9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY0MjE3NDM4NX0.C9KHQkWka3EvXLtxko6W5VA2o9FO0jME93TiPJIZw64/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C106%2C0%2C593&height=700\"/><br/><br/><ul><li>People who are intolerant of uncertainty are more likely to hold extreme beliefs, according to previous research.</li><li>A new study shows that this phenomenon seems to apply to both the left and the right. </li><li>The results are promising because they suggest political polarization might be mitigated by presenting information in a more neutral fashion and by helping people better tolerate uncertainty.</li></ul><hr/><p>In 2020, researchers at Stanford and Brown University published a study outlining how political polarization is accelerating quickly in most developed nations and especially in the U.S. The study aligned with <a href=\"https://bfi.uchicago.edu/insight/research-summary/elections-political-polarization-and-economic-uncertainty/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">prior research</a> showing that more Americans are reporting stronger negative feelings toward people in political parties other than their own. </p>\n<p>That may sound obvious. But what's more mysterious are the drivers of political polarization. Some blame news outlets, the <a href=\"https://www.vox.com/recode/21534345/polarization-election-social-media-filter-bubble\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">proliferation of social media</a>, economic downturns, or political figures.</p>\n<p>A new <a href=\"https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/33986114/\" target=\"_blank\">study</a> proposes a psychological hypothesis: The intolerance of uncertainty drives people to see the world through a partisan lens. In other words, people who see the world in black and white are more vulnerable to ideology. </p>\n<p><strong>Intolerance of uncertainty</strong></p>\n<p>Although previous research has proposed that <a href=\"https://spssi.onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/pdf/10.1111/josi.12022\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">uncertainty intolerance drives political polarization,</a> the new study is the first to show that this phenomenon seems to apply to both the left and the right. </p>\n<p>\"One theory posits that polarization arises because holding extreme political views satisfies a need for certain and stable beliefs about the world,\" the researchers wrote. \"This suggests that intolerance to uncertainty may play an outsized role in shaping polarized perceptions.\"</p>\n<p>The researchers invited 22 liberals and 22 conservatives to participate in a study for which they watched three video segments while fMRI recorded their brain activity. The segments included \"a neutrally worded news segment on a politically charged topic [abortion; PBS News], an inflammatory debate segment [police brutality and immigration; 2016 CNN Vice Presidential debate], and a nonpolitical nature video [BBC Earth].\"</p>\n<p>The participants then answered questions about their understanding of and reactions to the videos, political orientation, and tolerance of uncertainty.</p>\n        <p class=\"shortcode-media shortcode-media-rebelmouse-image\">\n        <img class=\"rm-shortcode\" data-rm-shortcode-id=\"41710f8bbdb979fcbeaf5215895170fb\" data-rm-shortcode-name=\"rebelmouse-image\" id=\"d8022\" loading=\"lazy\" src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjQwOTc3NC9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY2NDA2NTYzN30.uszOaXZ-g8oIXyVGLjb-myvD7KEgoaTw1G1NOJQ88-Y/img.jpg?width=980\"/>\n        \n        \n        <small class=\"image-media media-caption\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Caption...\">A. Participants underwent fMRI and behavioral testing as part of a larger study on political cognition. B. Participants viewed three videos in a fixed order while undergoing fMRI. C. Participants were clearly divided on political ideology. D. Analytical approach.</small><small class=\"image-media media-photo-credit\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Credit...\">Baar et al.</small></p>\n        <p>As uncertainty-intolerant people watched the inflammatory debate, their fMRI data showed remarkably similar activity in regions of the brain associated with social and emotional processing, including the bilateral temporoparietal junction (TPJ), right anterior insula (rAI), and precuneus. But there were differences based on party affiliation: Liberals' activity was synched with other liberals, while conservatives matched other conservatives.<br/></p>\n<p>\"This suggests that uncertainty-intolerant individuals see the political world through a stronger partisan lens, construing a more biased picture of the political reality,\" the researchers wrote. \"Rather than ideology alone, cognitive traits such as intolerance to uncertainty — which interact with ideology to form a polarized perception of the world — may be the linchpin of political polarization.\"</p>\n<p><strong>Hot-button topics don't have to be polarizing</strong></p>\n<p>Interestingly, the video segment on abortion didn't produce much neural synchrony.</p>\n<p>\"Even though abortion is a highly polarizing topic, the neutrally-worded news video yielded much less ideology-driven neural synchrony than the inflammatory debate video, which suggests that polarized perception is not just driven by ideological differences but also by the way polarizing issues are presented,\" the researchers wrote.</p>\n<p>That bit of the study is promising. It suggests that people might not be as inclined to succumb to ideology when information is presented in a more neutral, level-headed fashion. </p>\n<p><strong>Is partisanship a treatable mental health condition?</strong></p>\n<p>Also encouraging is the overall finding that uncertainty intolerance exacerbates the formation of rigid political beliefs. That's because uncertainty intolerance can theoretically be changed through <a href=\"https://www.anxietycanada.com/articles/intolerance-of-uncertainty/#:~:text=If%20you%20can't%20get,be%20more%20TOLERANT%20of%20uncertainty.\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">interventions like cognitive behavioral therapy</a>. </p>\n<p>Encouraging people to tolerate more uncertainty may steer them away from seeing the world through a thick ideological lens, which often \"hampers bipartisan cooperation and can even undermine the basic principles of democracy,\" the researchers wrote.</p>\n<p>Still, widespread social and economic unrest can understandably make that a difficult task. </p>\n<ul></ul><p>\"The growing uncertainty caused by large-scale societal events in the past year (e.g. job loss and a global pandemic) may fuel political polarization by sowing rigidly partisan perceptions of the world,\" the researchers wrote. \"Conversely, interventions against polarization may be successful by addressing citizens' sources of worry.\"</p><img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/BxEVfHmAzNE\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\"/>","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Wed, 19 May 2021 13:00:00 +0000","feedId":32,"bgimg":"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjQwOTc1Ni9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY0MjE3NDM4NX0.C9KHQkWka3EvXLtxko6W5VA2o9FO0jME93TiPJIZw64/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C106%2C0%2C593&height=700","linkMd5":"2b83904189b00403036fba7104c633f0","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx11/cdn47@2020_4/2021/05/19/14-56-01-198_dad742b49d05d61f.webp","destWidth":1245,"destHeight":700,"sourceBytes":75568,"destBytes":75568,"author":"Stephen Johnson","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjQwOTc1Ni9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY0MjE3NDM4NX0.C9KHQkWka3EvXLtxko6W5VA2o9FO0jME93TiPJIZw64/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C106%2C0%2C593&height=700":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx11/cdn47@2020_4/2021/05/19/14-56-01-198_dad742b49d05d61f.webp","https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjQwOTc3NC9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY2NDA2NTYzN30.uszOaXZ-g8oIXyVGLjb-myvD7KEgoaTw1G1NOJQ88-Y/img.jpg?width=980":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx24/cdn20@2020_4/2021/05/19/14-56-31-741_d802208b58160734.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/BxEVfHmAzNE":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx15/cdn50@2020_3/2021/05/19/14-56-31-912_a855c535378837fb.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1621436161069},{"createdTime":"2021-05-19 22:56:01","updatedTime":"2021-05-19 22:56:01","title":"'Ghost forests' visible from space spread along the coast as sea levels rise","link":"https://bigthink.com/surprising-science/ghost-forests","description":"<img src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjM4NzAzOS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYzNzUzMDI4NH0.RRTnRoAPOePgK6bHjaNNdIAhnCuzNfZMgyeRKoWUhYo/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C181%2C0%2C574&height=700\"/><br/><br/>Trekking out to my research sites near North Carolina's <a href=\"https://www.fws.gov/refuge/alligator_river/\" target=\"_blank\">Alligator River National Wildlife Refuge</a>, I slog through knee-deep water on a section of trail that is completely submerged.<hr/><p> Permanent flooding has become commonplace on this low-lying peninsula, nestled behind North Carolina's Outer Banks. The trees growing in the water are small and stunted. Many are dead. </p><p>Throughout coastal North Carolina, evidence of forest die-off is everywhere. Nearly every roadside ditch I pass while driving around the region is lined with dead or dying trees. </p><p>As an <a href=\"https://scholar.google.com/citations?user=uHGlqtEAAAAJ&hl=en\" target=\"_blank\">ecologist studying wetland response to sea level rise</a>, I know this flooding is evidence that climate change is altering landscapes along the Atlantic coast. It's emblematic of environmental changes that also threaten wildlife, ecosystems, and local farms and forestry businesses.  </p><p>Like all living organisms, trees die. But what is happening here is not normal. Large patches of trees are dying simultaneously, and saplings aren't growing to take their place. And it's not just a local issue: Seawater is raising salt levels in coastal woodlands along the entire Atlantic Coastal Plain, from Maine to Florida. Huge swaths of contiguous forest are dying. They're now known in the scientific community as “ghost forests.\" </p><p>            <a href=\"https://images.theconversation.com/files/377693/original/file-20210108-21-db4mka.jpg?ixlib=rb-1.1.0&q=45&auto=format&w=1000&fit=clip\"><img alt=\"\" sizes=\"(min-width: 1466px) 754px, (max-width: 599px) 100vw, (min-width: 600px) 600px, 237px\" src=\"https://images.theconversation.com/files/377693/original/file-20210108-21-db4mka.jpg?ixlib=rb-1.1.0&q=45&auto=format&w=754&fit=clip\" srcset=\"https://images.theconversation.com/files/377693/original/file-20210108-21-db4mka.jpg?ixlib=rb-1.1.0&q=45&auto=format&w=600&h=478&fit=crop&dpr=1 600w, https://images.theconversation.com/files/377693/original/file-20210108-21-db4mka.jpg?ixlib=rb-1.1.0&q=30&auto=format&w=600&h=478&fit=crop&dpr=2 1200w, https://images.theconversation.com/files/377693/original/file-20210108-21-db4mka.jpg?ixlib=rb-1.1.0&q=15&auto=format&w=600&h=478&fit=crop&dpr=3 1800w, https://images.theconversation.com/files/377693/original/file-20210108-21-db4mka.jpg?ixlib=rb-1.1.0&q=45&auto=format&w=754&h=601&fit=crop&dpr=1 754w, https://images.theconversation.com/files/377693/original/file-20210108-21-db4mka.jpg?ixlib=rb-1.1.0&q=30&auto=format&w=754&h=601&fit=crop&dpr=2 1508w, https://images.theconversation.com/files/377693/original/file-20210108-21-db4mka.jpg?ixlib=rb-1.1.0&q=15&auto=format&w=754&h=601&fit=crop&dpr=3 2262w\"/></a></p><p>              Deer photographed by a remote camera in a climate change-altered forest in North Carolina. Emily Ury, <a class=\"license\" href=\"http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nd/4.0/\">CC BY-ND</a></p><h2>The insidious role of salt</h2><p>Sea level rise <a href=\"https://www.ipcc.ch/srocc/chapter/chapter-4-sea-level-rise-and-implications-for-low-lying-islands-coasts-and-communities/\" target=\"_blank\">driven by climate change</a> is making wetlands wetter in many parts of the world. It's also making them saltier. </p><p>In 2016 I began working in a forested North Carolina wetland to study the effect of salt on its plants and soils. Every couple of months, I suit up in heavy rubber waders and a mesh shirt for protection from biting insects, and haul over 100 pounds of salt and other equipment out along the flooded trail to my research site. We are salting an area about the size of a tennis court, seeking to mimic the effects of sea level rise. </p><p>After two years of effort, the salt didn't seem to be affecting the plants or soil processes that we were monitoring. I realized that instead of waiting around for our experimental salt to slowly kill these trees, the question I needed to answer was how many trees had already died, and how much more wetland area was vulnerable. To find answers, I had to go to sites where the trees were already dead. </p><p>Rising seas are inundating North Carolina's coast, and saltwater is seeping into wetland soils. Salts move through groundwater during phases when freshwater is depleted, such as during droughts. Saltwater also moves through canals and ditches, penetrating inland with help from wind and high tides. Dead trees with pale trunks, devoid of leaves and limbs, are a telltale sign of high salt levels in the soil. A 2019 report called them “<a href=\"https://www.sciencefriday.com/videos/the-seeds-of-ghost-forests/\" target=\"_blank\">wooden tombstones</a>.\" </p><p>As the trees die, more salt-tolerant shrubs and grasses move in to take their place. In a newly published study that I coauthored with <a href=\"https://nicholas.duke.edu/people/faculty/bernhardt\" target=\"_blank\">Emily Bernhardt</a> and <a href=\"https://nicholas.duke.edu/people/faculty/wright\" target=\"_blank\">Justin Wright</a> at Duke University and <a href=\"https://evsc.as.virginia.edu/people/profile/xiyang\" target=\"_blank\">Xi Yang</a> at the University of Virginia, we show that in North Carolina <a href=\"https://doi.org/10.1002/eap.2339\" target=\"_blank\">this shift has been dramatic</a>. </p><p>The state's coastal region has suffered a rapid and widespread loss of forest,  with cascading impacts on wildlife, including the endangered <a href=\"https://www.fws.gov/southeast/wildlife/mammals/red-wolf/#:%7E:text=Today%2C%20only%20about%2020%20red,facilities%20throughout%20the%20United%20States.\" target=\"_blank\">red wolf</a> and <a href=\"https://www.fws.gov/rcwrecovery/files/rcwoodpecker.pdf\" target=\"_blank\">red-cockaded woodpecker</a>. Wetland forests <a href=\"https://doi.org/10.1088/1748-9326/aba136\" target=\"_blank\">sequester and store large quantities of carbon</a>, so forest die-offs also contribute to further climate change.</p><p>            <a href=\"https://images.theconversation.com/files/365100/original/file-20201022-19-1ygyqjg.PNG?ixlib=rb-1.1.0&q=45&auto=format&w=1000&fit=clip\"><img alt=\"\" sizes=\"(min-width: 1466px) 754px, (max-width: 599px) 100vw, (min-width: 600px) 600px, 237px\" src=\"https://images.theconversation.com/files/365100/original/file-20201022-19-1ygyqjg.PNG?ixlib=rb-1.1.0&q=45&auto=format&w=754&fit=clip\" srcset=\"https://images.theconversation.com/files/365100/original/file-20201022-19-1ygyqjg.PNG?ixlib=rb-1.1.0&q=45&auto=format&w=600&h=558&fit=crop&dpr=1 600w, https://images.theconversation.com/files/365100/original/file-20201022-19-1ygyqjg.PNG?ixlib=rb-1.1.0&q=30&auto=format&w=600&h=558&fit=crop&dpr=2 1200w, https://images.theconversation.com/files/365100/original/file-20201022-19-1ygyqjg.PNG?ixlib=rb-1.1.0&q=15&auto=format&w=600&h=558&fit=crop&dpr=3 1800w, https://images.theconversation.com/files/365100/original/file-20201022-19-1ygyqjg.PNG?ixlib=rb-1.1.0&q=45&auto=format&w=754&h=701&fit=crop&dpr=1 754w, https://images.theconversation.com/files/365100/original/file-20201022-19-1ygyqjg.PNG?ixlib=rb-1.1.0&q=30&auto=format&w=754&h=701&fit=crop&dpr=2 1508w, https://images.theconversation.com/files/365100/original/file-20201022-19-1ygyqjg.PNG?ixlib=rb-1.1.0&q=15&auto=format&w=754&h=701&fit=crop&dpr=3 2262w\"/></a></p><p>              Researcher Emily Ury measuring soil salinity in a ghost forest. Emily Bernhardt, <a class=\"license\" href=\"http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nd/4.0/\">CC BY-ND</a></p><h2>Assessing ghost forests from space</h2><p>To understand where and how quickly these forests are changing, I needed a bird's-eye perspective. This perspective comes from satellites like <a href=\"https://eospso.nasa.gov/mission-category/3\" target=\"_blank\">NASA's Earth Observing System</a>, which are important sources of scientific and environmental data. </p><p>            <a href=\"https://images.theconversation.com/files/373173/original/file-20201205-15-pwkhva.PNG?ixlib=rb-1.1.0&q=45&auto=format&w=1000&fit=clip\"><img alt=\"Satellite image of coastal North Carolina\" sizes=\"(min-width: 1466px) 754px, (max-width: 599px) 100vw, (min-width: 600px) 600px, 237px\" src=\"https://images.theconversation.com/files/373173/original/file-20201205-15-pwkhva.PNG?ixlib=rb-1.1.0&q=45&auto=format&w=237&fit=clip\" srcset=\"https://images.theconversation.com/files/373173/original/file-20201205-15-pwkhva.PNG?ixlib=rb-1.1.0&q=45&auto=format&w=600&h=417&fit=crop&dpr=1 600w, https://images.theconversation.com/files/373173/original/file-20201205-15-pwkhva.PNG?ixlib=rb-1.1.0&q=30&auto=format&w=600&h=417&fit=crop&dpr=2 1200w, https://images.theconversation.com/files/373173/original/file-20201205-15-pwkhva.PNG?ixlib=rb-1.1.0&q=15&auto=format&w=600&h=417&fit=crop&dpr=3 1800w, https://images.theconversation.com/files/373173/original/file-20201205-15-pwkhva.PNG?ixlib=rb-1.1.0&q=45&auto=format&w=754&h=524&fit=crop&dpr=1 754w, https://images.theconversation.com/files/373173/original/file-20201205-15-pwkhva.PNG?ixlib=rb-1.1.0&q=30&auto=format&w=754&h=524&fit=crop&dpr=2 1508w, https://images.theconversation.com/files/373173/original/file-20201205-15-pwkhva.PNG?ixlib=rb-1.1.0&q=15&auto=format&w=754&h=524&fit=crop&dpr=3 2262w\"/></a></p><p>              A 2016 Landsat8 image of the Albemarle Pamlico Peninsula in coastal North Carolina. <a class=\"source\" href=\"https://earth.google.com/web/@35.5254573,-76.51525198,-32.07608694a,254931.95954248d,35y,0h,0t,0r\">USGS</a></p><p>Since 1972, <a href=\"https://www.usgs.gov/core-science-systems/nli/landsat\" target=\"_blank\">Landsat satellites</a>, jointly operated by NASA and the U.S. Geological Survey, have captured <a href=\"https://eros.usgs.gov/image-gallery/image-of-the-week/landsat-archive-hits-nine-million-scenes\" target=\"_blank\">continuous images of Earth's land surface</a> that reveal both natural and human-induced change. We used Landsat images to quantify changes in coastal vegetation since 1984 and referenced high-resolution Google Earth images to spot ghost forests. Computer analysis helped identify  similar patches of dead trees across the entire landscape.</p><p>            <a href=\"https://images.theconversation.com/files/365083/original/file-20201022-15-r94e2q.PNG?ixlib=rb-1.1.0&q=45&auto=format&w=1000&fit=clip\"><img alt=\"Google Earth image with road dividing healthy and dead forests.\" sizes=\"(min-width: 1466px) 754px, (max-width: 599px) 100vw, (min-width: 600px) 600px, 237px\" src=\"https://images.theconversation.com/files/365083/original/file-20201022-15-r94e2q.PNG?ixlib=rb-1.1.0&q=45&auto=format&w=754&fit=clip\" srcset=\"https://images.theconversation.com/files/365083/original/file-20201022-15-r94e2q.PNG?ixlib=rb-1.1.0&q=45&auto=format&w=600&h=414&fit=crop&dpr=1 600w, https://images.theconversation.com/files/365083/original/file-20201022-15-r94e2q.PNG?ixlib=rb-1.1.0&q=30&auto=format&w=600&h=414&fit=crop&dpr=2 1200w, https://images.theconversation.com/files/365083/original/file-20201022-15-r94e2q.PNG?ixlib=rb-1.1.0&q=15&auto=format&w=600&h=414&fit=crop&dpr=3 1800w, https://images.theconversation.com/files/365083/original/file-20201022-15-r94e2q.PNG?ixlib=rb-1.1.0&q=45&auto=format&w=754&h=520&fit=crop&dpr=1 754w, https://images.theconversation.com/files/365083/original/file-20201022-15-r94e2q.PNG?ixlib=rb-1.1.0&q=30&auto=format&w=754&h=520&fit=crop&dpr=2 1508w, https://images.theconversation.com/files/365083/original/file-20201022-15-r94e2q.PNG?ixlib=rb-1.1.0&q=15&auto=format&w=754&h=520&fit=crop&dpr=3 2262w\"/></a></p><p>              Google Earth image of a healthy forest on the right and a ghost forest with many dead trees on the left. <a class=\"source\" href=\"https://www.google.com/maps/@35.8604723,-75.7949324,219m/data=!3m1!1e3\">Emily Ury</a></p><p>The results were shocking. We found that more than 10% of forested wetland within the Alligator River National Wildlife Refuge was lost over the past 35 years. This is federally protected land, with no other human activity that could be killing off the forest. </p><p><a href=\"https://coast.noaa.gov/slr/\">Rapid sea level rise</a> seems to be outpacing the ability of these forests to adapt to wetter, saltier conditions. <a href=\"https://www.carbonbrief.org/mapped-how-climate-change-affects-extreme-weather-around-the-world\" target=\"_blank\">Extreme weather events</a>, fueled by climate change, are causing further damage from heavy storms, more frequent hurricanes and drought. </p><p>We found that the largest annual loss of forest cover within our study area occurred in 2012, following a period of extreme drought, forest fires and storm surges from <a href=\"https://www.weather.gov/mhx/Aug272011EventReview\" target=\"_blank\">Hurricane Irene</a> in August 2011. This triple whammy seemed to have been a tipping point that caused mass tree die-offs across the region. </p><p>            <a href=\"https://images.theconversation.com/files/365089/original/file-20201022-19-l3nopz.jpg?ixlib=rb-1.1.0&q=45&auto=format&w=1000&fit=clip\"><img alt=\"Salt water intrusion is rapidly killing North Carolina coastal forests.\" sizes=\"(min-width: 1466px) 754px, (max-width: 599px) 100vw, (min-width: 600px) 600px, 237px\" src=\"https://images.theconversation.com/files/365089/original/file-20201022-19-l3nopz.jpg?ixlib=rb-1.1.0&q=45&auto=format&w=754&fit=clip\" srcset=\"https://images.theconversation.com/files/365089/original/file-20201022-19-l3nopz.jpg?ixlib=rb-1.1.0&q=45&auto=format&w=600&h=338&fit=crop&dpr=1 600w, https://images.theconversation.com/files/365089/original/file-20201022-19-l3nopz.jpg?ixlib=rb-1.1.0&q=30&auto=format&w=600&h=338&fit=crop&dpr=2 1200w, https://images.theconversation.com/files/365089/original/file-20201022-19-l3nopz.jpg?ixlib=rb-1.1.0&q=15&auto=format&w=600&h=338&fit=crop&dpr=3 1800w, https://images.theconversation.com/files/365089/original/file-20201022-19-l3nopz.jpg?ixlib=rb-1.1.0&q=45&auto=format&w=754&h=424&fit=crop&dpr=1 754w, https://images.theconversation.com/files/365089/original/file-20201022-19-l3nopz.jpg?ixlib=rb-1.1.0&q=30&auto=format&w=754&h=424&fit=crop&dpr=2 1508w, https://images.theconversation.com/files/365089/original/file-20201022-19-l3nopz.jpg?ixlib=rb-1.1.0&q=15&auto=format&w=754&h=424&fit=crop&dpr=3 2262w\"/></a></p><p>              Habitat maps we created for the Alligator River National Wildlife Refuge showing the change over time and the prevalence of ghost forests. <a class=\"source\" href=\"https://doi.org/10.1002/eap.2339\" target=\"_blank\">Ury et al, 2021.</a>, <a class=\"license\" href=\"http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nd/4.0/\" target=\"_blank\">CC BY-ND</a></p><h2>Should scientists fight the transition or assist it?</h2><p>As global sea levels continue to rise, coastal woodlands from the <a href=\"https://earthobservatory.nasa.gov/images/8357/katrina-damage-to-gulf-coast-forests\" target=\"_blank\">Gulf of Mexico</a> to <a href=\"https://gwtoday.gwu.edu/chesapeake-bay-ghost-forests-highlight-wetland-transformation#:%7E:text=In%20the%20Chesapeake%20Bay%20region,lapping%20at%20their%20bleached%20trunks.\" target=\"_blank\">the Chesapeake Bay</a> and elsewhere around the world could also <a href=\"https://doi.org/10.1002/ehs2.1258\" target=\"_blank\">suffer major losses</a> from saltwater intrusion. Many people in the conservation community are rethinking land management approaches and exploring more <a href=\"https://www.landscapearchitecture.nz/landscape-architecture-aotearoa/2018/9/7/adaptive-planning-for-coastal-climate-change\" target=\"_blank\">adaptive strategies</a>, such as facilitating forests' inevitable transition into salt marshes or other coastal landscapes.</p><p>For example, in North Carolina <a href=\"https://coastalresilience.org/project/north-carolina/\" target=\"_blank\">the Nature Conservancy</a> is carrying out some adaptive management approaches, such as <a href=\"https://oceanservice.noaa.gov/facts/living-shoreline.html#:%7E:text=Living%20shorelines%20are%20a%20green,sills%20to%20stabilize%20the%20shoreline.\" target=\"_blank\">creating “living shorelines</a>\" made from plants, sand and rock to provide natural buffering from storm surges.</p><p>A more radical approach would be to introduce marsh plants that are salt-tolerant in threatened zones. This strategy is controversial because it goes against the desire to try to preserve ecosystems exactly as they are.</p><p>But if forests are dying anyway, having a salt marsh is a far better outcome than allowing a wetland to be reduced to open water. While open water isn't inherently bad, it does not provide the many ecological benefits that a salt marsh affords. Proactive management may prolong the lifespan of coastal wetlands, enabling them to continue storing carbon, providing habitat, enhancing water quality and protecting productive farm and forest land in coastal regions.<img alt=\"The Conversation\" height=\"1\" src=\"https://counter.theconversation.com/content/147971/count.gif?distributor=republish-lightbox-basic\" width=\"1\"/></p><p><a href=\"https://theconversation.com/profiles/emily-ury-1160085\" target=\"_blank\">Emily Ury</a>, Ph.D. Candidate, <em><a href=\"https://theconversation.com/institutions/duke-university-1286\" target=\"_blank\">Duke University</a></em></p><p>This article is republished from <a href=\"https://theconversation.com\" target=\"_blank\">The Conversation</a> under a Creative Commons license.
