article</a>.</p><img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/sdrpr6cxbzk\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\"/>","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Thu, 13 May 2021 08:34:41 +0000","feedId":32,"bgimg":"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjM4NzAzOS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYzNzUzMDI4NH0.RRTnRoAPOePgK6bHjaNNdIAhnCuzNfZMgyeRKoWUhYo/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C181%2C0%2C574&height=700","linkMd5":"7896d9a3f907485e14c3afc52a7bc0d9","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx29/cdn64@2020_6/2021/05/19/14-56-01-250_d1419e2eb35d4bc5.webp","destWidth":1245,"destHeight":701,"sourceBytes":268708,"destBytes":268708,"author":"Emily Ury","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjM4NzAzOS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYzNzUzMDI4NH0.RRTnRoAPOePgK6bHjaNNdIAhnCuzNfZMgyeRKoWUhYo/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C181%2C0%2C574&height=700":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx29/cdn64@2020_6/2021/05/19/14-56-01-250_d1419e2eb35d4bc5.webp","https://images.theconversation.com/files/377693/original/file-20210108-21-db4mka.jpg?ixlib=rb-1.1.0&q=45&auto=format&w=754&fit=clip":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx12/cdn47@2020_4/2021/05/19/14-56-32-377_a1e2b8ed5eabc53b.webp","https://images.theconversation.com/files/365100/original/file-20201022-19-1ygyqjg.PNG?ixlib=rb-1.1.0&q=45&auto=format&w=754&fit=clip":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx8/cdn6@2020_4/2021/05/19/14-56-31-780_da3b2cacac363880.webp","https://images.theconversation.com/files/373173/original/file-20201205-15-pwkhva.PNG?ixlib=rb-1.1.0&q=45&auto=format&w=237&fit=clip":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx19/cdn15@2020_6/2021/05/19/14-56-31-705_034ae43355201088.webp","https://images.theconversation.com/files/365083/original/file-20201022-15-r94e2q.PNG?ixlib=rb-1.1.0&q=45&auto=format&w=754&fit=clip":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx22/cdn45@2020_3/2021/05/19/14-56-31-818_418a575ac0ebfc39.webp","https://images.theconversation.com/files/365089/original/file-20201022-19-l3nopz.jpg?ixlib=rb-1.1.0&q=45&auto=format&w=754&fit=clip":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx3/cdn39@2020_2/2021/05/19/14-56-32-188_9cdaffaf3e2500fe.webp","https://counter.theconversation.com/content/147971/count.gif?distributor=republish-lightbox-basic":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx24/cdn32@2020_6/2021/05/19/14-56-32-575_392b4bb96f315e6d.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/sdrpr6cxbzk":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx2/cdn59@2020_3/2021/05/19/14-56-31-665_4ef800df3dd5d29f.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1621436161075},{"createdTime":"2021-05-19 22:56:01","updatedTime":"2021-05-19 22:56:01","title":"The Great Resignation: COVID revealed how abnormal the modern workplace is","link":"https://bigthink.com/culture-religion/great-resignation-abnormal-modern-workplace","description":"<img src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjM5ODkxNC9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYyOTM2Njg1M30.8hwUv16Lv9yYGCp0-I9U71Tlisx5V4wKRz5Q0nta_RE/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C220%2C0%2C220&height=700\"/><br/><br/><ul> <li>The Great Resignation is an idea proposed by Professor Anthony Klotz that predicts a large number of people leaving their jobs after the COVID pandemic ends and life returns to \"normal.\"</li><li>French philosopher Michel Foucault argued that by establishing what is and is not \"normal,\" we are exerting a kind of power by making people behave a certain way.</li><li>If working from home becomes the new normal, we must be careful that it doesn't give way to a new lifestyle that we hate even more than the office.</li></ul><hr/><p>You wake up, you put on your work clothes, and you go to the office. You sit behind a desk, or in some designated space, and you work until the clock says it's over. This is what life is like for the vast majority of people. That is, until COVID came along. Then, everything changed.</p><p>Recently, an interesting idea has emerged called the \"Great Resignation.\" This is a phenomenon that Professor Anthony Klotz of Texas A&M University has predicted will happen when people are asked, or told, to return to their offices. Klotz argues that, when we're all forced back into the old reality of the commute, a nine-to-five job, and cubicle life, there will be a \"Great Resignation\" among the workforce. </p><p>The argument is that in times of uncertainty and insecurity — like during a global pandemic — people behave conservatively. They'll stay put. But once things \"normalize\" again, we ought to expect employees to head for the exits.</p><p>But why? What has changed? Why has working from home made us so dissatisfied with our previously normal lives? Other than the comfort and convenience of working from home, one explanation might involve the concept of \"normalization,\" a topic that fascinated French philosopher Michel Foucault.</p><h2>The power of normal people</h2><p>Foucault argued that we often spend an inordinate amount of time trying to be normal. We must dress the same way as everyone else. We must talk about the same things. We must work just like everyone else works. It's hugely important that things are normal. But, behind all of this, is a power dynamic that many of us are simply unaware of — and unconsciously unhappy about. </p><p>Someone, somewhere, must define what is \"normal.\" It is then for the rest of us to bend over backward to fit into this narrow mold. To be powerful, then, is to say, \"Do this, otherwise everyone will call you weird.\" Power is to hold the hoops everyone else must jump through. It's what Foucault describes as \"normalizing power.\"</p><h2>COVID was a wake-up call to the abnormality of modern work</h2><p class=\"shortcode-media shortcode-media-rebelmouse-image\">\n        <img class=\"rm-shortcode\" data-rm-shortcode-id=\"2b0a1565a46e3a7cf1003a5948d23fb0\" data-rm-shortcode-name=\"rebelmouse-image\" id=\"a6195\" loading=\"lazy\" src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjM5ODkyMi9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY1NDk2MzU2M30.1P2RqoNmbotbH9lmQRBqGZqTSZ4fwGGsGDXZBV39Q88/img.jpg?width=980\"/>\n        \n        \n        <small class=\"image-media media-photo-credit\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Credit...\"><a href=\"https://www.gettyimages.com/detail/news-photo/chinese-commuters-wear-protective-masks-as-they-exit-a-news-photo/1230831262?adppopup=true\" target=\"_blank\">Credit</a>: <a href=\"https://www.gettyimages.com/search/photographer?family=editorial&photographer=Lintao+Zhang\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">Lintao Zhang</a> via Getty Images</small></p><p>Let's apply Focault's normalization concept to the modern workplace. Accepted wisdom had it that the best — and really, the <em>only</em> way — to work was in an office, usually downtown, far away from where we live. We were told this is where collaboration and creativity occur. Largely unchallenged, this \"normal\" functioned for decades, and we all obeyed.<br/></p><p>We had to wake up at the crack of dawn to get ready for work. We had to travel in clogged and joyless commutes. We had to eat ready-packaged lunches behind our too-small desks. We had to sit through meetings in \"good posture\" ergonomic chairs that wouldn't be out of place in the Spanish Inquisition. Then we had to travel back home in yet another clogged and joyless commute. And we did this day after day after day.</p><p>Then COVID came along and revealed just how artificial, unnecessary, and <em>ab</em>normal it all is. It's as if someone ripped a blindfold off of society. We have laptops, wi-fi, and 5G (at least when people aren't burning the towers down). Many of us were just as productive — if not <em>more</em> so — than during the \"normal\" pre-COVID era. We don't need to be in an office. We don't need to waste countless hours of our lives sitting in traffic. </p><blockquote>While the idea of a Great Resignation is quite appealing right now, we should be careful the \"new normal\" isn't so much worse.</blockquote><p>Even better, people got to spend more time with their families, enjoy long and restful breaks, and have space to pursue their hobbies. In short, people like not going to an office. And, as Klotz argues, when companies see this dissatisfaction — this Great Resignation — they're going to ask some revolutionary questions, like, \"Do you want to come back full time? Work remotely? In-office three days a week? Four days? One day?\"</p><p>The silver lining to the COVID pandemic is that it has made us re-examine what \"normal\" is.</p><h2>Beware the new normal</h2><p>Of course, the idea of a nine-to-five office job was not established by some moustache-twirling villain just to satisfy his sadistic whims. It came about because people thought that was the most effective and productive way to operate.</p><p>People do need direct human contact, and it's often easier and more productive to speak to a colleague next to you or walk across an office to ask for some help. Remote-working software like Zoom is indeed convenient, but can a company honestly say that it's as efficient as working in an office?</p><p>What's more, there's a particularly pernicious sting in what Foucault argued. It's something that ought to slow any would-be Great Resignation. This is the idea that there likely will <em>always</em> be some kind of normal. </p><p>While COVID has revealed the office for the normalized power play that it is, what's to say what the next \"normal\" will be? Let's say that working from home becomes the new normal. Will we be expected to attend Zoom meetings at any hour of the day or answer text messages at midnight? Might cameras be used to monitor our every movement? Might software check that we're working at the right pace and in the right way?</p><ul></ul><p>While the idea of a Great Resignation is quite appealing right now, we should be careful the \"new normal\" isn't so much worse.</p><img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/f5dcvIo_oCY\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\"/>","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Mon, 17 May 2021 15:00:00 +0000","feedId":32,"bgimg":"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjM5ODkxNC9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYyOTM2Njg1M30.8hwUv16Lv9yYGCp0-I9U71Tlisx5V4wKRz5Q0nta_RE/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C220%2C0%2C220&height=700","linkMd5":"004e91166588d27de5c768e94b682c8b","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx23/cdn75@2020_6/2021/05/19/14-56-01-197_9dd3bbcab298de58.webp","destWidth":1245,"destHeight":700,"sourceBytes":48586,"destBytes":48586,"author":"Jonny Thomson","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjM5ODkxNC9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYyOTM2Njg1M30.8hwUv16Lv9yYGCp0-I9U71Tlisx5V4wKRz5Q0nta_RE/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C220%2C0%2C220&height=700":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx23/cdn75@2020_6/2021/05/19/14-56-01-197_9dd3bbcab298de58.webp","https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjM5ODkyMi9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY1NDk2MzU2M30.1P2RqoNmbotbH9lmQRBqGZqTSZ4fwGGsGDXZBV39Q88/img.jpg?width=980":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx8/cdn20@2020_3/2021/05/19/14-56-32-107_a619507e19dc4640.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/f5dcvIo_oCY":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx25/cdn61@2020_5/2021/05/19/14-56-31-807_bf5afbc31e650f64.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1621436161068},{"createdTime":"2021-05-19 22:56:01","updatedTime":"2021-05-19 22:56:01","title":"Why professional soccer players choke during penalty kicks","link":"https://bigthink.com/mind-brain/choking-under-pressure","description":"<img src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjM3OTkxMC9vcmlnaW4ucG5nIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY3OTA4MzAwOH0.CtJZy2xZiONIN4Y0GrrsKLMpJ7YpZ9VnyYA9_UddPgc/img.png?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C31%2C0%2C19&height=700\"/><br/><br/><ul>  <li>The new study is the first to use in-the-field imaging technology to measure brain activity as people delivered penalty kicks.</li><li>Participants were asked to kick a total of 15 penalty shots under three different scenarios, each designed to be increasingly stressful.</li><li>Kickers who missed shots showed higher activity in brain areas that were irrelevant to kicking a soccer ball, suggesting they were overthinking.</li></ul><hr/><p>In a 2019 soccer match, Swansea City was down 1-0 against West Brom late in the first half. A penalty was called against West Brom. Swansea midfielder Bersant Celina was preparing to deliver a penalty kick. He scuttled up to the ball, but his foot only made partial contact, lobbing it weakly to the right.</p><p>Was it a simple mistake? Maybe. But there might be deeper explanations for why professional athletes choke under high-pressure situations. </p><p>A new study published in <a href=\"https://www.frontiersin.org/articles/10.3389/fcomp.2021.661466/full\" target=\"_blank\"><em>Frontiers in Computer Science</em></a> used functional near-infrared spectroscopy (fNIRS) to analyze the brain activity of inexperienced and experienced soccer players as they missed penalty shots. Although past research has explored why soccer players miss penalty shots, the recent study is the first to do so using in-the-field fNIRS measurement.</p><p>The results showed that kickers who choked were activating parts of their brain associated with long-term thinking, self-instruction, and self-reflection. The chokers, in other words, were overthinking it.</p><p><strong>The psychology of penalty kicks</strong></p><p>Penalty shots offer an interesting case study of how mental pressure affects physical performance. After all, there's a lot at stake, not only because the kick can sometimes render a win or loss, but also because there are sometimes millions of people anxiously watching, some of whom might have a financial interest in the outcome. </p><p>That pressure is no joke. For example, research on Men's World Cup penalty shoot-outs has shown that when the score is tied and a goal means an immediate win, <a href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/2010/05/31/sports/soccer/31penaltykicks.html?pagewanted=1&_r=1&src=un&feedurl=http://json8.nytimes.com/pages/sports/soccer/index.jsonp\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">players score 92 percent of kicks</a>. But when teams are facing elimination in a shootout, and the kick determines an immediate tie or loss, players only score 60 percent of the time.</p><p style=\"margin-left: 20px;\">\"How can it be that football players with a near perfect control over the ball (they can very precisely kick a ball over more than 50 meters) fail to score a penalty kick from only 11 meters?\" study co-author Max Slutter, of the University of Twente in the Netherlands, said in a <a href=\"https://www.eurekalert.org/pub_releases/2021-05/f-htt042821.php\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">press release</a>. </p><p style=\"margin-left: 20px;\">\"Obviously, huge psychological pressure plays a role, but why does this pressure cause a missed penalty? We tried to answer this by measuring the brain activity of football players during the physical execution of a penalty kick.\"</p><p>In the new study, the researchers aimed to answer two key questions about choking under pressure among both experienced and inexperienced players: (1) What is the difference in brain activity between success (scoring) and failure (missing) when taking a penalty kick? (2) What brain activity is associated with performing under pressure during a penalty kick situation?</p><p>To find out, the researchers asked ten experienced soccer players and twelve inexperienced players to participate in a penalty-kicking task. The task was divided into three rounds, each of which was designed to be increasingly stressful:</p><ul><li>Round 1 had no goalkeeper and was labeled as a practice round.</li><li>Round 2 had a friendly goalkeeper who wasn't allowed to distract the kicker.</li><li>Round 3 had a competitive goalkeeper who was allowed to distract the kicker, and kickers were also competing for a prize.</li></ul><p>Participants kicked five shots in each round. They wore a fNIRS-equipped headset during the task that measured activity in various parts of the brain.</p><p>All participants performed worse in the second and third rounds and reported experiencing the most pressure in the third round. Inexperienced players performed worse than experienced players, which might suggest that they were less able to deal with the mental stress.</p><p class=\"shortcode-media shortcode-media-rebelmouse-image\">\n        <img class=\"rm-shortcode\" data-rm-shortcode-id=\"a423891ae5cdf10022e5f79a29cd25c5\" data-rm-shortcode-name=\"rebelmouse-image\" id=\"b6ac1\" loading=\"lazy\" src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjM3OTkwMi9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY2MTMzODQ2N30.2J04vkFbN-qFA95BEwLmSHLJARC9RvFqgo4r651e12o/img.jpg?width=980\"/>\n        \n        \n        <small class=\"image-media media-caption\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Caption...\">The locations in which experienced and inexperienced players kicked the ball in each round. Red dots represent missed penalties and green dots represent scored penalties.Slutter et al., Frontiers in Computer Science, 2021.</small></p><p><strong>The neuroscience of choke artists<br/></strong></p><p>So, what types of brain activity were associated with missed shots? </p><p>The most noticeable result was that kickers missed more shots when they showed higher activity in their prefrontal cortex (PFC), an area of the brain associated with long-term planning. This was especially true among participants who reported higher levels of anxiety. More specifically, experienced soccer players who missed shots showed high activity in the left temporal cortex, which is related to self-instruction and self-reflection. </p><p style=\"margin-left: 20px;\">\"By activating the left temporal cortex more, experienced players neglect their automated skills and start to <em>overthink</em> the situation,\" the researchers wrote. \"This increase can be seen as a distracting factor.\"</p><p>Also, when players of all experience levels felt anxious and missed shots, they showed less activity in the motor cortex, which is the brain area most directly associated with kicking a penalty shot. </p><p><strong>Don't overthink it</strong></p><p>The results suggest that mental pressure can activate parts of the brain that are irrelevant to the task at hand. In general, expert athletes show more efficient brain activity — that is, more activity in relevant areas, and less activity in irrelevant areas — and therefore experience fewer distractions. This is likely one reason why they were more successful at penalties than inexperienced players in high-stress situations.</p><p>This principle is described by <a href=\"https://www.frontiersin.org/articles/10.3389/fnbeh.2017.00072/full#:~:text=%E2%80%9CNeural%20efficiency%E2%80%9D%20hypothesis%20posits%20that,sports%20unrelated%20visuo%2Dspatial%20tasks.\" target=\"_blank\">neural efficiency theory</a>, and it applies not only to athletes but experts in any field. As you gain mastery over something, you can rely more on automatic brain processes rather than deliberate thinking, which can lead to distractions. The authors of the study concluded that their results provide supporting evidence for neural efficiency theory.</p><p>Still, as long our experts are human, it seems that high-pressure situations can turn anyone into a choke artist.</p><div></div><ul></ul><div></div><img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/L4ep6886Nk0\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\"/>","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Wed, 12 May 2021 17:00:02 +0000","feedId":32,"bgimg":"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjM3OTkxMC9vcmlnaW4ucG5nIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY3OTA4MzAwOH0.CtJZy2xZiONIN4Y0GrrsKLMpJ7YpZ9VnyYA9_UddPgc/img.png?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C31%2C0%2C19&height=700","linkMd5":"1e2b5a0a61f988c8bd5efade3ed7f3d0","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx5oss/cdn79@2020_5/2021/05/19/14-56-01-186_dd9be2a04c67f106.webp","destWidth":1245,"destHeight":701,"sourceBytes":48152,"destBytes":48152,"author":"Stephen Johnson","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjM3OTkxMC9vcmlnaW4ucG5nIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY3OTA4MzAwOH0.CtJZy2xZiONIN4Y0GrrsKLMpJ7YpZ9VnyYA9_UddPgc/img.png?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C31%2C0%2C19&height=700":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx5oss/cdn79@2020_5/2021/05/19/14-56-01-186_dd9be2a04c67f106.webp","https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjM3OTkwMi9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY2MTMzODQ2N30.2J04vkFbN-qFA95BEwLmSHLJARC9RvFqgo4r651e12o/img.jpg?width=980":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx19/cdn27@2020_2/2021/05/19/14-56-32-360_b6ac18a18f96337d.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/L4ep6886Nk0":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx27/cdn47@2020_3/2021/05/19/14-56-31-702_304d93b0906cb91c.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1621436161066},{"createdTime":"2021-05-19 22:56:01","updatedTime":"2021-05-19 22:56:01","title":"Study: ADHD is overdiagnosed and overtreated","link":"https://bigthink.com/surprising-science/adhd-overdiagnosed-children","description":"<img src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjQwNTU5OS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY2MjU1MTQxMH0.eiPsrl_icy328o95ELmueekRqu-pM7AOBLJvSVX9O_U/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C108%2C0%2C210&height=700\"/><br/><br/><ul><li>ADHD is an extremely contentious disorder in terms of diagnosis and treatment.</li><li><span></span>A research team examined 334 studies on ADHD published between 1979 and 2020.</li><li>The team concluded that ADHD is being overdiagnosed and overtreated in children with milder symptoms.</li></ul><hr/><ul></ul><p>Attention deficit hyperactivity disorder (ADHD) has long been a controversial topic. While the term \"mental restlessness\" <a href=\"https://books.google.com/books?id=OMAtAAAAYAAJ\" target=\"_blank\">dates back</a> to 1798, English pediatrician George Still described the disorder in front of the Royal College of Physicians of London in 1902. The condition is attributed to both nature and nurture, with a <a href=\"https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC6481311/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">recent study</a> suggesting the disorder is 75 percent genetic. </p><p>According to <a href=\"https://www.nimh.nih.gov/health/publications/attention-deficit-hyperactivity-disorder-adhd-the-basics/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">DSM-IV criteria</a>, ADHD affects five to seven percent of children; but according to <a href=\"https://books.google.com/books?id=O3sSd-OAdP0C\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">ICD-10</a>, only between one and two percent are afflicted. Global estimates <a href=\"https://www.thelancet.com/pb-assets/Lancet/gbd/summaries/diseases/adhd.pdf\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">state</a> that nearly 85 million people suffer from ADHD, which, like autism, exists on a spectrum. </p><p>Treatment is perhaps the most contentious issue. While a holistic approach includes counseling, lifestyle changes, and medication, due to insurance requirements and other factors, many children only receive the latter. And now a new <a href=\"https://jamanetwork.com/journals/jamanetworkopen/fullarticle/2778451\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">systematic scoping review</a> published in the journal <em>JAMA Network Open</em> that investigated 334 studies conducted between 1979 and 2020 found that ADHD is being both overdiagnosed and overtreated in children and adolescents. </p><h2>ADHD: An epidemic of overdiagnosis</h2><p>Researchers from the University of Sydney and the Institute for Evidence-Based Healthcare in Australia initially retrieved 12,267 relevant studies before using a set of criteria that whittled the list down to 334. Only five studies critically investigated the costs and benefits of treating milder cases of ADHD, prompting the team to focus on knowledge gaps in side effects. </p><p>The team writes that public scrutiny has increased along with the increase in diagnoses. The numbers are startling: between 1997 and 2016, the number of children reported to be suffering from ADHD <a href=\"https://jamanetwork.com/journals/jamanetworkopen/fullarticle/2698633\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">doubled</a>. While the symptoms of ADHD include fidgeting, inattention, and impulsivity, Dr. Stephen Hinshaw compared this disorder to depression, as neither condition has \"unequivocal biological markers.\" He <a href=\"https://khn.org/news/over-past-20-years-the-percentage-of-children-with-adhd-nearly-doubles/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">continues</a>, \"It's probably not a true epidemic of ADHD. It might be an epidemic of diagnosing it.\"</p><p>The Australian researchers write that ambiguous or mild symptoms might contribute to diagnostic inflation and the subsequent rise in the prevalence of ADHD. They compare this to cancer, a field that has established protocols for overdiagnosis. ADHD is still understudied in this regard.</p><p class=\"shortcode-media shortcode-media-rebelmouse-image\">\n        <img class=\"rm-shortcode\" data-rm-shortcode-id=\"790cc091ad013a5f8e837731f04ced31\" data-rm-shortcode-name=\"rebelmouse-image\" id=\"fb220\" loading=\"lazy\" src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjQwNTYwOC9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYzNjkyNjI1NH0.XSAlOfoOaVthqp-zxRiA09rQjEozDH3r2yFKDLGGUIo/img.jpg?width=980\"/>\n        \n        \n        <small class=\"image-media media-photo-credit\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Credit...\">Photo: fizkes / Adobe Stock</small></p><h2>Overdiagnosis is harmful<br/></h2><p>This has contributed to an increase in potential harm, not just to children's health (such as the long-term pharmacological impact on developing brains) but to parents' finances. As of 2018, ADHD is a <a href=\"https://www.grandviewresearch.com/industry-analysis/attention-deficit-hyperactivity-disorder-adhd-market\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">$16.4 billion global industry</a>, with continued revenue growth predicted — ensured by future ADHD diagnoses. </p><p>The costs and benefits of ADHD treatment are mixed. The authors write:</p><p style=\"margin-left: 20px;\">\"We found evidence of benefits for academic outcomes, injuries, hospital admissions, criminal behavior, and quality of life. In addition, harmful outcomes were evident for heart rate and cardiovascular events, growth and weight, risk for psychosis and tics, and stimulant misuse or poisoning.\"</p><p>For most of these studies, the benefits outweighed the risks in children suffering from more severe ADHD. But this is not true for children with milder symptoms. </p><p>Across the studies, the team noticed that four themes emerged. The first two were positive, and the second two were negative:</p><ol><li>For some people, an ADHD diagnosis was shown to create a sense of empowerment because a biological explanation provided a sense of legitimacy.</li><li>Feelings of empowerment enabled help-seeking behavior.</li><li>For others, a biomedical explanation led to disempowerment because it served as an excuse and provided a way to shirk responsibility. </li><li>An ADHD diagnosis was linked to stigmatization and social isolation.</li></ol><ul></ul><p>The unfortunate reality is that ADHD is a real condition that should be treated in some children. But for many, the harm of treatment outweighs the benefits.</p><p>--</p><ul></ul><ul></ul><p><em>Stay in touch with Derek on <a href=\"http://www.twitter.com/derekberes\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">Twitter</a> and <a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/DerekBeresdotcom\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">Facebook</a>. His most recent book is</em> \"<em><a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/gp/product/B08KRVMP2M?pf_rd_r=MDJW43337675SZ0X00FH&pf_rd_p=edaba0ee-c2fe-4124-9f5d-b31d6b1bfbee\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">Hero's Dose: The Case For Psychedelics in Ritual and Therapy</a>.\"</em></p><img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/zkwNkdU213M\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\"/>","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Tue, 18 May 2021 13:00:00 +0000","feedId":32,"bgimg":"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjQwNTU5OS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY2MjU1MTQxMH0.eiPsrl_icy328o95ELmueekRqu-pM7AOBLJvSVX9O_U/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C108%2C0%2C210&height=700","linkMd5":"09ebedee9e4c0201a8b89f82e4fef415","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx14/cdn71@2020_1/2021/05/19/14-56-01-179_e2dd4bfe71dc370d.webp","destWidth":1245,"destHeight":700,"sourceBytes":70168,"destBytes":70168,"author":"Derek Beres","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjQwNTU5OS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY2MjU1MTQxMH0.eiPsrl_icy328o95ELmueekRqu-pM7AOBLJvSVX9O_U/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C108%2C0%2C210&height=700":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx14/cdn71@2020_1/2021/05/19/14-56-01-179_e2dd4bfe71dc370d.webp","https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjQwNTYwOC9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYzNjkyNjI1NH0.XSAlOfoOaVthqp-zxRiA09rQjEozDH3r2yFKDLGGUIo/img.jpg?width=980":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx31/cdn55@2020_2/2021/05/19/14-56-31-846_fb220444f7c04185.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/zkwNkdU213M":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx29/cdn78@2020_4/2021/05/19/14-56-31-662_b76d517e72a89429.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1621436161062},{"createdTime":"2021-05-19 22:56:01","updatedTime":"2021-05-19 22:56:01","title":"Epicurus and the atheist's guide to happiness","link":"https://bigthink.com/personal-growth/epicureanism-greek-philosophy-atheists-happiness","description":"<img src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjM5MDM3NS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY0NjQ2Mjk1NX0.cg40UW7hALELaK4-_Sj9xugk4h_iu56C30hCXPmsvQA/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C423%2C0%2C85&height=700\"/><br/><br/><ul> <li>The Epicureans were some of the world's first materialists and argued that there is neither God, nor gods, nor spirits, but only atoms and the physical world.</li><li>They believed that life was about finding pleasure and avoiding pain and that both were achieved by minimizing our desires for things. </li><li>The Epicurean Four Step Remedy is advice on how we can face the world, achieve happiness, and not worry as much as we do.</li></ul><hr/><p><span></span>Self-help books are consistently on the best-seller lists across the world. We can't seem to get enough of happiness advice, wellness gurus, and life coaches. But, as the Book of Ecclesiastes says, there is nothing new under the sun. The Ancient Greeks were into the self-help business millennia before the likes of Dale Carnegie and Mark Manson.</p><h2>Four schools of ancient Greek philosophy</h2><p>From the 3rd century BCE until the birth of Jesus, Greek philosophy was locked into an ideological war. Four rival schools emerged, each proclaiming loudly that they — alone — had the secret to a happy and fulfilled life. These schools were: Stoicism, Cynicism, Skepticism, and Epicureanism. Each had their advocates and even had a kind of PR battle to get people to sign up to their side. They were trying to sell happiness.</p><blockquote>Epicurus's guide to living is noticeably different from a lot of modern self-help books in just how little day-to-day advice it gives. </blockquote><p>Many of us are familiar with Stoicism, a topic I covered <a href=\"https://bigthink.com/personal-growth/stoicism-help-you-stop-worrying\" target=\"_self\">recently</a>, because it forms the foundation of cognitive behavioral therapy. Skepticism and Cynicism have become watered down or warped variations of their original forms. (I will cover these in future articles.) Today, we focus on the most underappreciated of these schools, the Epicureans. In their philosophy, we can find a surprisingly modern and easy-to-follow \"Four Part Remedy\" to life.</p><h2>Epicureans: The first atheists</h2><p>The Epicureans were some of history's first materialists.<a href=\"https://bigthink.com/culture-religion/ancient-physics-democritus-predicted-atom\" target=\"_self\"> They believed that the world was made up only of atoms (and void)</a>, and that everything is simply a particular composition of these atoms. There were no gods, spirits, or souls (or, at most, they're irrelevant to the world as we encounter it). They thought that there was no afterlife or immortality to be had, either. Death is just a relocation of atoms. This atheism and materialism was what the Christian Church would later come to despise, and after centuries of being villainized by priests, popes, and church doctrine, the Epicureans fell out of fashion. </p><p>In the atomistic, worldly philosophy of the Epicureans, all there is to life is to get as much pleasure as you can and avoid pain. This isn't to become some rampant hedonist, staggering from opium dens to brothels, but concerns the <em>higher</em> pleasures of the mind.</p><p>Epicurus, himself, believed that pleasure was defined as the satisfying of a desire, such as when we drink a glass of water when we're really thirsty. But, he also argued that desires themselves were painful since they, by definition, meant longing and anguish. Thirst is a desire, and we don't like being thirsty. True contentment, then, could not come from creating and indulging pointless wants but must instead come from minimizing desire altogether. What would be the point of setting ourselves new targets? These are just new desires that we must make efforts to satisfy. Thus, minimizing pain meant minimizing desires, and the bare minimum desires were those required to live.</p><h2>The Four Part Remedy</h2><p class=\"shortcode-media shortcode-media-rebelmouse-image\">\n        <img class=\"rm-shortcode\" data-rm-shortcode-id=\"8771623602bf5ff714564d29ed7255bb\" data-rm-shortcode-name=\"rebelmouse-image\" id=\"fb439\" loading=\"lazy\" src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjM5MDQwNC9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYzMTI1MjI1Mn0.tmMK8sogwVrcqKAf8vISJWntYcsDnAI8AgicZkZwA_0/img.jpg?width=980\"/>\n        \n        \n        <small class=\"image-media media-photo-credit\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Credit...\"><a href=\"https://www.gettyimages.com/detail/news-photo/people-listen-to-a-speech-by-a-philosopher-during-the-xxiii-news-photo/175741687?adppopup=true\" target=\"_blank\">Credit</a>: <a href=\"https://www.gettyimages.com/search/photographer?family=editorial&photographer=LOUISA+GOULIAMAKI\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">LOUISA GOULIAMAKI</a> via Getty Images</small></p><p>Given that Epicureans were determined to maximize pleasure and minimize pain, they developed a series of rituals and routines designed to help. One of the best known (not least because we've lost so much written by the Epicureans) was the so-called \"Four Part Remedy.\" These were four principles they believed we ought to accept so that we might find solace and be rid of existential and spiritual pain:<br/></p><p><strong>1. Don't fear God. </strong>Remember, everything is just atoms. You won't go to hell, and you won't go to heaven. The \"afterlife\" will be nothingness, in just the same way as when you had no awareness whatsoever of the dinosaurs or Cleopatra. There was simply nothing before you existed, and death is a great expanse of the same timeless, painless void.</p><p><strong>2. Don't worry about death. </strong>This is a natural corollary of Step 1. With no body, there is no pain. In death, we lose all of our desires and, along with them, suffering and discontent. It's striking how <a href=\"https://bigthink.com/culture-religion/axial-age-greatest-minds-walked-earth\" target=\"_self\">similar in tone this sounds</a> to a lot of Eastern, especially Buddhist, philosophy at the time.</p><p><strong>3. What is good is easy to get. </strong>Pleasure comes in satisfying desires, specifically the basic, biological desires required to keep us alive. Anything more complicated than this, or harder to achieve, just creates pain. There's water to be drunk, food to be eaten, and beds to sleep in. That's all you need.</p><p><strong>4. What is terrible is easy to endure. </strong>Even if it is difficult to satisfy the basic necessities, remember that pain is short-lived. We're rarely hungry for long, and sicknesses most often will be cured easily enough (and this was written 2300 years before antibiotics). All other pains often can be mitigated by pleasures to be had. If basic biological necessities can't be met, then you die — but we already established there is nothing to fear from death.</p><h2>Practical Epicureanism</h2><p>Epicurus's guide to living is noticeably different from a lot of modern self-help books in just how little day-to-day advice it gives. It doesn't tell us \"the five things you need to do before breakfast\" or \"visit these ten places, and you'll never be sad again.\" Just like it's rival school of <a href=\"https://bigthink.com/personal-growth/stoicism-help-you-stop-worrying\" target=\"_self\">Stoicism</a>, Epicureanism is all about a psychological shift of some kind.</p><p>Namely, that psychological shift is about recognizing that life doesn't need to be as complicated as we make it. At the end of the day, we're just animals with basic needs. We have the tools necessary to satisfy our desires, but when we don't, we have huge reservoirs of strength and resilience capable of enduring it all. Failing that, we still have nothing to fear because there is nothing to fear about death. When we're alive, death is nowhere near; when we're dead, we won't care.</p><ul></ul><p>Practical, modern, and straightforward, Epicurus offers a valuable insight to life. It's existential comfort for the materialists and atheists. It's happiness in four lines.</p><p><em>Jonny Thomson teaches philosophy in Oxford. He runs a popular Instagram account called Mini Philosophy (@</em><a href=\"https://www.instagram.com/philosophyminis/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"><em>philosophyminis</em></a><em>). His first book is </em><a href=\"https://www.amazon.co.uk/Mini-Philosophy-Small-Book-Ideas-ebook/dp/B08M3XDNPM/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"><em>Mini Philosophy: A Small Book of Big Ideas</em></a><em>.</em></p><img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/Sv1vOWdH2NA\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\"/>","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Fri, 14 May 2021 13:00:00 +0000","feedId":32,"bgimg":"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjM5MDM3NS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY0NjQ2Mjk1NX0.cg40UW7hALELaK4-_Sj9xugk4h_iu56C30hCXPmsvQA/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C423%2C0%2C85&height=700","linkMd5":"ae64329449c6910283edc0b36034545b","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx12/cdn19@2020_3/2021/05/19/14-56-01-215_cf2c52f37128c6c9.webp","destWidth":1245,"destHeight":700,"sourceBytes":34042,"destBytes":34042,"author":"Jonny Thomson","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjM5MDM3NS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY0NjQ2Mjk1NX0.cg40UW7hALELaK4-_Sj9xugk4h_iu56C30hCXPmsvQA/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C423%2C0%2C85&height=700":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx12/cdn19@2020_3/2021/05/19/14-56-01-215_cf2c52f37128c6c9.webp","https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjM5MDQwNC9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYzMTI1MjI1Mn0.tmMK8sogwVrcqKAf8vISJWntYcsDnAI8AgicZkZwA_0/img.jpg?width=980":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx23/cdn89@2020_4/2021/05/19/14-56-31-759_fb43998cc65e5dc3.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/Sv1vOWdH2NA":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx14/cdn99@2020_6/2021/05/19/14-56-32-200_df2f71d9b6e2fb08.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1621436161071},{"createdTime":"2021-05-19 22:56:01","updatedTime":"2021-05-19 22:56:01","title":"Looking for something? A team at MIT develop a robot that sees through walls","link":"https://bigthink.com/technology-innovation/robot-sees-through-walls","description":"<img src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjQwNDAwNi9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY2MzY3NjAxNH0.jfkrASmGNemi29QaHbGm2tA8zylnO5tlFb-5PZpuhOA/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C96%2C0%2C96&height=700\"/><br/><br/>In recent years, robots have gained artificial vision, touch, and even smell.<hr/><p> \"Researchers have been giving robots human-like perception,\" says MIT Associate Professor Fadel Adib. In a new paper, Adib's team is pushing the technology a step further. \"We're trying to give robots superhuman perception,\" he says.</p><p>The researchers have developed a robot that uses radio waves, which can pass through walls, to sense occluded objects. The robot, called RF-Grasp, combines this powerful sensing with more traditional computer vision to locate and grasp items that might otherwise be blocked from view. The advance could one day streamline e-commerce fulfillment in warehouses or help a machine pluck a screwdriver from a jumbled toolkit.</p><p>The research will be presented in May at the IEEE International Conference on Robotics and Automation. The paper's lead author is Tara Boroushaki, a research assistant in the Signal Kinetics Group at the MIT Media Lab. Her MIT co-authors include Adib, who is the director of the Signal Kinetics Group; and Alberto Rodriguez, the Class of 1957 Associate Professor in the Department of Mechanical Engineering. Other co-authors include Junshan Leng, a research engineer at Harvard University, and Ian Clester, a PhD student at Georgia Tech.</p><a rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"></a><img alt=\"Video thumbnail\" src=\"https://i1.ytimg.com/vi/ZAzeYPcTM78/maxresdefault.jpg\"/><a target=\"_blank\">Play video</a><p><br/></p><p>As e-commerce continues to grow, warehouse work is still usually the domain of humans, not robots, despite sometimes-dangerous working conditions. That's in part because robots struggle to locate and grasp objects in such a crowded environment. \"Perception and picking are two roadblocks in the industry today,\" says Rodriguez. Using optical vision alone, robots can't perceive the presence of an item packed away in a box or hidden behind another object on the shelf — visible light waves, of course, don't pass through walls.</p><p>But radio waves can.</p><p>For decades, radio frequency (RF) identification has been used to track everything from library books to pets. RF identification systems have two main components: a reader and a tag. The tag is a tiny computer chip that gets attached to — or, in the case of pets, implanted in — the item to be tracked. The reader then emits an RF signal, which gets modulated by the tag and reflected back to the reader.</p><p>The reflected signal provides information about the location and identity of the tagged item. The technology has gained popularity in retail supply chains — Japan aims to use RF tracking for nearly all <a href=\"https://www.forbes.com/sites/akikokatayama/2019/02/26/japan-aims-to-automate-all-convenience-stores-by-2025-with-a-new-rfid-technology/?sh=34ab85f7745b\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">retail purchases</a> in a matter of years. The researchers realized this profusion of RF could be a boon for robots, giving them another mode of perception.</p><p>\"RF is such a different sensing modality than vision,\" says Rodriguez. \"It would be a mistake not to explore what RF can do.\"</p><p>RF Grasp uses both a camera and an RF reader to find and grab tagged objects, even when they're fully blocked from the camera's view. It consists of a robotic arm attached to a grasping hand. The camera sits on the robot's wrist. The RF reader stands independent of the robot and relays tracking information to the robot's control algorithm. So, the robot is constantly collecting both RF tracking data and a visual picture of its surroundings. Integrating these two data streams into the robot's decision making was one of the biggest challenges the researchers faced.</p><p>\"The robot has to decide, at each point in time, which of these streams is more important to think about,\" says Boroushaki. \"It's not just eye-hand coordination, it's RF-eye-hand coordination. So, the problem gets very complicated.\"</p><p>The robot initiates the seek-and-pluck process by pinging the target object's RF tag for a sense of its whereabouts. \"It starts by using RF to focus the attention of vision,\" says Adib. \"Then you use vision to navigate fine maneuvers.\" The sequence is akin to hearing a siren from behind, then turning to look and get a clearer picture of the siren's source.</p><p>With its two complementary senses, RF Grasp zeroes in on the target object. As it gets closer and even starts manipulating the item, vision, which provides much finer detail than RF, dominates the robot's decision making.</p><p>RF Grasp proved its efficiency in a battery of tests. Compared to a similar robot equipped with only a camera, RF Grasp was able to pinpoint and grab its target object with about half as much total movement. Plus, RF Grasp displayed the unique ability to \"declutter\" its environment — removing packing materials and other obstacles in its way in order to access the target. Rodriguez says this demonstrates RF Grasp's \"unfair advantage\" over robots without penetrative RF sensing. \"It has this guidance that other systems simply don't have.\"</p><p>RF Grasp could one day perform fulfilment in packed e-commerce warehouses. Its RF sensing could even instantly verify an item's identity without the need to manipulate the item, expose its barcode, then scan it. \"RF has the potential to improve some of those limitations in industry, especially in perception and localization,\" says Rodriguez.</p><p>Adib also envisions potential home applications for the robot, like locating the right Allen wrench to assemble your Ikea chair. \"Or you could imagine the robot finding lost items. It's like a super-Roomba that goes and retrieves my keys, wherever the heck I put them.\"</p><p>The research is sponsored by the National Science Foundation, NTT DATA, Toppan, Toppan Forms, and the Abdul Latif Jameel Water and Food Systems Lab (J-WAFS).</p><p>Reprinted with permission of <a href=\"http://news.mit.edu/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">MIT News</a>. Read the <a href=\"https://news.mit.edu/2021/robot-senses-hidden-objects-0401\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">original article</a>.</p><img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/FACsZFxZpiY\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\"/>","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Mon, 17 May 2021 16:50:08 +0000","feedId":32,"bgimg":"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjQwNDAwNi9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY2MzY3NjAxNH0.jfkrASmGNemi29QaHbGm2tA8zylnO5tlFb-5PZpuhOA/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C96%2C0%2C96&height=700","linkMd5":"57b974b7a1ec5620ecc4c277c7a92b5b","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx4/cdn100@2020_4/2021/05/19/14-56-01-213_c0d5038a01d6a340.webp","destWidth":1245,"destHeight":700,"sourceBytes":57204,"destBytes":57204,"author":"Daniel Ackerman | MIT News Office","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjQwNDAwNi9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY2MzY3NjAxNH0.jfkrASmGNemi29QaHbGm2tA8zylnO5tlFb-5PZpuhOA/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C96%2C0%2C96&height=700":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx4/cdn100@2020_4/2021/05/19/14-56-01-213_c0d5038a01d6a340.webp","https://i1.ytimg.com/vi/ZAzeYPcTM78/maxresdefault.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx10/cdn80@2020_2/2021/05/19/14-56-31-727_fe75dbb4d6d6b00b.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/FACsZFxZpiY":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx30/cdn28@2020_2/2021/05/19/14-56-31-659_4b33fb7b2df5fc1b.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1621436161066},{"createdTime":"2021-05-19 22:56:01","updatedTime":"2021-05-19 22:56:01","title":"30 grunts and sounds that may have been the first language","link":"https://bigthink.com/surprising-science/30-iconic-sounds","description":"<img src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjM5ODUyMy9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY1MDg3NzIyNX0.yTnTivstlqZvrCC1boYsNU_BHrobDrM0lveT1ehVJH4/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C0%2C0%2C0&height=700\"/><br/><br/><ul><li>What did the first person who wanted to speak say?</li><li><span></span>New research suggests that there are lots of sounds that everyone understands.</li><li>These sounds may have allowed the first exchanges that gave birth to language.</li></ul><hr/><p>As hard as it is sometimes to get a conversation started, imagine how difficult it must have been before words existed. Linguists have long wondered how verbal language began. <em>Some</em> form of communication must have been in place to get the whole thing going. Maybe it was gestures.</p><p>Now, a new study published in <a href=\"https://www.nature.com/articles/s41598-021-89445-4\" target=\"_blank\"><em>Scientific Reports</em></a> by linguists at the University of Birmingham (UBir) in the UK and the Leibniz-Centre General Linguistics in Berlin proposes another idea: Verbal communication may have begun at least partly with \"iconic\" mouth-produced sounds whose meanings were inherently obvious to anyone who heard them. (The researchers use the word \"iconic\" to mean that these sounds represent things.)</p><p>The importance of these sounds may also extend beyond their role as the ultimate conversation starters, <a href=\"https://www.birmingham.ac.uk/news/latest/2021/05/ancestors-created-'iconic'-sounds.aspx\" target=\"_blank\">says</a> co-author Marcus Perlman of UBir. \"Our study fills in a crucial piece of the puzzle of language evolution, suggesting the possibility that all languages — spoken as well as signed — may have iconic origins.\"</p><h2>30 iconic sounds</h2><p><br/></p><p class=\"shortcode-media shortcode-media-rebelmouse-image\">\n        <img class=\"rm-shortcode\" data-rm-shortcode-id=\"68ec306e400aaef148adaaca763f6a67\" data-rm-shortcode-name=\"rebelmouse-image\" id=\"3e697\" loading=\"lazy\" src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjM5ODUyNS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY0OTIzMTMwNX0.1Dho40BMs9i8ikoo5LrLNVZdtOGhElYh-Z1K7qN0kQ4/img.jpg?width=980\"/>\n        \n        \n        <small class=\"image-media media-photo-credit\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Credit...\">Credit: <a href=\"https://stock.adobe.com/contributor/332708/alexander-pokusay?load_type=author&prev_url=detail\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">Alexander Pokusay</a> /</small></p><p>The researchers have posted a few of these iconic sounds: \"<a href=\"https://mfr.osf.io/render?url=https://osf.io/ejzr9/?direct%26mode=render%26action=download%26mode=render\" target=\"_blank\">cut</a>,\" \"<a href=\"https://mfr.osf.io/render?url=https://osf.io/euwyn/?direct%26mode=render%26action=download%26mode=render\" target=\"_blank\">tiger</a>,\" \"<a href=\"https://mfr.osf.io/render?url=https://osf.io/85ysk/?direct%26mode=render%26action=download%26mode=render\" target=\"_blank\">water</a>,\" and \"<a href=\"https://mfr.osf.io/render?url=https://osf.io/7wrcy/?direct%26mode=render%26action=download%26mode=render\" target=\"_blank\">good</a>.\" (Note: These audio files won't play in Apple's Safari browser.) The study reveals that there are a lot more of these sounds than previously appreciated, and likely enough to form a bridge to language development.<br/></p><p>Co-author UBir's Bodo Winter explains:</p><p>\"Our findings challenge the often-cited idea that vocalizations have limited potential for iconic representation, demonstrating that in the absence of words people can use vocalizations to communicate a variety of meanings — serving effectively for cross-cultural communication when people lack a common language.\"</p><p>The researchers compiled a list of 30 iconic-sound candidates that likely would have been of use to the earliest speakers. These included mouth noises that could represent:</p><ul><li>animate beings — \"child,\" \"man,\" \"woman,\" \"snake,\" \"tiger,\" \"deer\"</li><li>inanimate objects — \"fire,\" \"rock,\" \"meat,\" \"water,\" \"knife,\" \"fruit\"</li><li>activities — \"eat,\" \"sleep,\" \"cut,\" \"cook,\" \"gather,\" \"hunt,\" \"hide\"</li><li>descriptors — \"good,\" \"bad,\" \"small,\" \"big\" \"dull,\" \"sharp\"</li><li>quantities — \"one,\" \"many\"</li><li>demonstrative words — \"this,\" \"that\"</li></ul><h2>Was \"nom, nom\" the sound for eating?</h2><p><br/></p><p class=\"shortcode-media shortcode-media-rebelmouse-image\">\n        <img class=\"rm-shortcode\" data-rm-shortcode-id=\"2467378a6958226b8941ecd44e3e28cd\" data-rm-shortcode-name=\"rebelmouse-image\" id=\"51cb0\" loading=\"lazy\" src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjM5ODUyNi9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYzNTU2NDA4OX0.iCSE_ysC3eVzpS9uLY6daERpK_ivlU9taq3_aE3KSy8/img.jpg?width=980\"/>\n        \n        \n        <small class=\"image-media media-photo-credit\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Credit...\">Credit: Aleksandra Ćwiek, et al. / <a href=\"https://www.nature.com/articles/s41598-021-89445-4/figures/1\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">Scientific Reports</a></small></p><p>Making a list — and making noises — is one thing; finding out if anyone understands them is another. The researchers tested out their iconic sounds in two different experiments.<br/></p><p>In an online experiment, speakers of 25 different languages were asked to match the meaning of iconic sounds to six written labels. They listened to three performances for each of the 30 candidates, 90 recordings in all.</p><p>Participants correctly identified the sounds' meaning roughly 65 percent of the time.</p><p>Some meanings were more readily understood than others. \"Sleep\" was correctly identified by almost 99 percent, as opposed to \"that,\" understood by only 35 percent. The most often understood sounds were \"eat,\" \"child,\" \"sleep,\" \"tiger,\" and \"water.\" The least? \"That,\" \"gather,\" \"sharp,\" \"dull,\" and \"knife.\" </p><p>The researchers next conducted field experiments to capture the meaningfulness of the sounds in oral cultures with inconsistent literacy levels. For these people, researchers played twelve iconic sounds for animals and inanimate objects as listeners identified each from a grid of pictures. The volunteers correctly identified the sounds' meanings about 56 percent of the time, again above the level of chance.</p><h2>The universal roots of language</h2><p>In addition to being the sounds that facilitated the birth of language, the authors of the study wonder if such commonly understood sounds may also be a factor in the similarities that exist between different modern languages that don't share a common root language. They cite other <a href=\"https://doi.org/10.1177/1754073919897295\" target=\"_blank\">research</a> that found \"vocalizations for 25 different emotions were identifiable across cultures with above-chance accuracy.\" </p><p>\"The ability to use iconicity to create universally understandable vocalizations,\" says Perlman, \"may underpin the vast semantic breadth of spoken languages, playing a role similar to representational gestures in the formation of signed languages.\"</p><img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/jnLyA17XiGs\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\"/>","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Mon, 17 May 2021 13:00:00 +0000","feedId":32,"bgimg":"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjM5ODUyMy9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY1MDg3NzIyNX0.yTnTivstlqZvrCC1boYsNU_BHrobDrM0lveT1ehVJH4/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C0%2C0%2C0&height=700","linkMd5":"efc70cb020a931b361822924227c62ea","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx2/cdn43@2020_4/2021/05/19/14-56-01-303_a8c3871a43de4e2a.webp","destWidth":1245,"destHeight":700,"sourceBytes":52432,"destBytes":52432,"author":"Robby Berman","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjM5ODUyMy9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY1MDg3NzIyNX0.yTnTivstlqZvrCC1boYsNU_BHrobDrM0lveT1ehVJH4/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C0%2C0%2C0&height=700":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx2/cdn43@2020_4/2021/05/19/14-56-01-303_a8c3871a43de4e2a.webp","https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjM5ODUyNS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY0OTIzMTMwNX0.1Dho40BMs9i8ikoo5LrLNVZdtOGhElYh-Z1K7qN0kQ4/img.jpg?width=980":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx12/cdn35@2020_2/2021/05/19/14-56-31-746_3e697d6543c3d5a6.webp","https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjM5ODUyNi9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYzNTU2NDA4OX0.iCSE_ysC3eVzpS9uLY6daERpK_ivlU9taq3_aE3KSy8/img.jpg?width=980":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx32/cdn35@2020_4/2021/05/19/14-56-32-202_51cb0d5406fdabd7.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/jnLyA17XiGs":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx22/cdn33@2020_3/2021/05/19/14-56-31-659_f8b65483eda2816d.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1621436161069},{"createdTime":"2021-05-19 22:56:01","updatedTime":"2021-05-19 22:56:01","title":"7 most notorious and excessive Roman Emperors","link":"https://bigthink.com/politics-current-affairs/7-most-notorious-and-excessive-roman-emperors","description":"<img src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjQwMDM2Ni9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY2MDM0ODY4M30.Q4-YRSknnKXauRGXOTFI4iiYtzR9_hqu06PHWjTixDQ/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C0%2C62%2C0&height=700\"/><br/><br/><ul><li><em></em>Roman Emperors were known for their excesses and violent behavior. </li><li>From Caligula to Elagabalus, the emperors exercised total power in the service of their often-strange desires.</li><li>Most of these emperors met violent ends themselves.</li></ul><p>We rightfully complain about many of our politicians and leaders today, but historically speaking, humanity has seen much worse. Arguably no set of rulers has been as debauched, ingenious in their cruelty, and prone to excess as the Roman Emperors.</p><p>While this list is certainly not exhaustive, here are seven Roman rulers who were perhaps the worst of the worst in what was one of the largest empires that ever existed, lasting for over a thousand years.</p><p><strong>1. Caligula</strong> </p><p>Officially known as Gaius (Gaius Caesar Augustus Germanicus), Caligula was the third Roman Emperor, ruling from 37 to 41 AD. He acquired the nickname \"Caligula\" (meaning \"little [soldier's] boot\") from his father's soldiers during a campaign. </p><p>While recognized for some positive measures in the early days of his rule, he became famous throughout the ages as an absolutely insane emperor, who killed anyone when it pleased him, spent exorbitantly, was obsessed with perverse sex, and proclaimed himself to be a living god.</p><p class=\"shortcode-media shortcode-media-rebelmouse-image\">\n        <img class=\"rm-shortcode\" data-rm-shortcode-id=\"ea5704ed5b012f0c31ad8982369c2383\" data-rm-shortcode-name=\"rebelmouse-image\" id=\"55fff\" loading=\"lazy\" src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjQwMDQxMi9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY0NTIyMTM1Nn0.GeQc_PgS0n-kXbM9aEZOan_Xr-ym5VDkUAYwnISOI8I/img.jpg?width=980\"/>\n        \n        \n        <small class=\"image-media media-caption\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Caption...\">Caligula gives his horse Incitatus a drink during a banquet. </small><small class=\"image-media media-photo-credit\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Credit...\">Credit: An engraving by Persichini from a drawing by Pinelli, from \"The History of the Roman Emperors\" from Augustus to Constantine, by Jean Baptiste Louis Crevier. 1836. </small></p><p>Among his litany of misdeeds, according to the accounts of Caligula's contemporaries<a href=\"http://www.earlychristianwritings.com/yonge/book40.html\" target=\"_blank\"> Philo</a> of Alexandria and Seneca the Younger, he slept with whomever he wanted, brazenly taking other men's wives (even on their wedding nights) and publicly talking about it. </p><p>He also had an insatiable blood thirst, killing for mere amusement. Once, as <a href=\"https://facultystaff.richmond.edu/~wstevens/history331texts/caligula.html\" target=\"_blank\">reports historian Suetonius</a>, when the bridge across the sea at Puteoli was being blessed, he had a number of spectators who were there to inspect it thrown off into the water. When some tried to cling to the ships' rudders, Caligula had them dislodged with hooks and oars so they would drown. On another occasion, he got so bored that he had his guards throw a whole section of the audience into the arena during the intermission so they would be eaten by wild beasts. He also allegedly executed two consuls who forgot his birthday.</p><p>Suetonius relayed further atrocities of the mad emperor's character, <a href=\"https://facultystaff.richmond.edu/~wstevens/history331texts/caligula.html\" target=\"_blank\">writing</a> that Caligula \"frequently had trials by torture held in his presence while he was eating or otherwise enjoying himself; and kept an expert headsman in readiness to decapitate the prisoners brought in from gaol.\" One particular form of torture associated with Caligula involved having people sawed in half. </p><p>He caused mass starvation and purposefully wasted money and resources, like making his troops stage fake battles just for theater. If that wasn't enough, he turned his palace into a brothel and was accused of incest with his sisters, Agrippina the Younger, Drusilla, and Livilla, whom he also prostituted to other men. Perhaps most famously, he was planning to appoint his favorite horse Incitatus a consul and<a href=\"https://penelope.uchicago.edu/Thayer/E/Roman/Texts/Cassius_Dio/59*.html#28\" target=\"_blank\"> went as far</a> as making the horse into a priest. </p><p>In early 41 AD, Caligula was assassinated by a conspiracy of Praetorian Guard officers, senators, and other members of the court.</p><p><strong>2. Nero</strong> </p><p>Fully named Nero Claudius Caesar, Nero ruled from 54 to 68 AD and was arguably an even worse madman than his uncle Caligula. He had his step-brother Britannicus killed, his wife Octavia executed, and his mother Agrippina stabbed and murdered. He personally kicked to death his lover Poppeaea while she was pregnant with his child — a horrific action the Roman historian Tacitus <a href=\"https://www.history.com/topics/ancient-history/nero\" target=\"_blank\">depicted</a> as \"a casual outburst of rage.\" </p><p>He spent exorbitantly and built a 100-foot-tall bronze statue of himself called the Colossus Neronis.</p><p>He is also remembered for being strangely obsessed with music. He sang and played the lyre, although it's not likely he really fiddled as Rome burned in what is a popular myth about this crazed tyrant. As misplaced retribution for the fire which burned down a sizable portion of Rome in the year 64, he executed scores of early Christians, some of them outfitted in animal skins and brutalized by dogs, with others burned at the stake.</p><p>He died by suicide.</p><p class=\"shortcode-media shortcode-media-rebelmouse-image\">\n        <img class=\"rm-shortcode\" data-rm-shortcode-id=\"720a98da44cc02c0c1b194298dcf1ffe\" data-rm-shortcode-name=\"rebelmouse-image\" id=\"05411\" loading=\"lazy\" src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjQwMDM4Mi9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY0ODY3Mzk4OH0.6lumF7a24TxnlGpMOl4tP_Kal9siW4yHNxwR4rzatHY/img.jpg?width=980\"/>\n        \n        \n        <small class=\"image-media media-caption\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Caption...\">Roman Emperor Nero in the burning ruins of Rome. July 64 AD.</small><small class=\"image-media media-photo-credit\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Credit...\">Credit: From an original painting by S.J. Ferris. (Photo by Kean Collection / <a href=\"https://www.gettyimages.com/detail/news-photo/roman-emperor-nero-stands-amid-the-ruins-of-rome-as-it-news-photo/501328749\" target=\"_blank\">Getty Images</a>)</small></p><p><strong>3. Commodus</strong><br/></p><p>Like some of his counterparts, Commodus (a.k.a. Lucius Aelius Aurelius Commodus) thought he was a god — in his case, a reincarnation of the Greek demigod Hercules. Ruling from 176 to 192 AD, he was also known for his debauched ways and strange stunts that seemed designed to affirm his divine status. Numerous statues around the empire showed him as Hercules, a warrior who fought both men and beasts. He fought hundreds of exotic animals in an arena like a gladiator, confusing and terrifying his subjects. Once, he killed 100 lions in a single day.</p><p class=\"shortcode-media shortcode-media-rebelmouse-image\">\n        <img class=\"rm-shortcode\" data-rm-shortcode-id=\"a50d81c2860a2bc16a4a15259d114134\" data-rm-shortcode-name=\"rebelmouse-image\" id=\"ff0f8\" loading=\"lazy\" src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjQwMDM3Ni9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY3OTU3NDkyNn0.7fgPZxVRBsk-BDamgJQPuSRbj1XyZ_6XNMOZJXjKqIY/img.jpg?width=980\"/>\n        \n        \n        <small class=\"image-media media-caption\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Caption...\">Emperor Commodus (Joaquin Phoenix) questions the loyalty of his sister Lucilla (Connie Nielsen) In Dreamworks Pictures' and Universal Pictures' Oscar-winning drama \"Gladiator,\" directed by Ridley Scott.</small><small class=\"image-media media-photo-credit\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Credit...\">Credit: <a href=\"https://www.gettyimages.com/detail/news-photo/the-emperor-of-rome-commodus-has-reason-to-question-the-news-photo/51103254\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">Photo By Getty Images</a></small></p><p>The burning desire to kill living creatures as a gladiator for the New Year's Day celebrations in 193 AD brought about his demise. After Commodus shot hundreds of animals with arrows and javelins every morning as part of the Plebeian Games leading up to New Year's, his fitness coach (aptly named Narcissus), choked the emperor to death in his bath.<br/></p><p><strong>4. Elagabalus</strong> </p><p>Officially named Marcus Aurelius Antoninus II, Elagabalus's nickname comes from his priesthood in the cult of the Syrian god Elagabal. Ruling as emperor from 218 to 222 AD, he was so devoted to the cult, which he tried to spread in Rome, that he had himself circumcised to prove his dedication. He further offended the religious sensitivities of his compatriots by essentially replacing the main Roman god Jupiter with Elagabal as the chief deity. In another nod to his convictions, he installed on Palatine Hill a cone-like fetish made of black stone as a symbol of the Syrian sun god Sol Invictus Elagabalus.</p><p>His sexual proclivities were also not well received at the time. He was likely transgender (wearing makeup and wigs), had five marriages, and was quite open about his male lovers. <a href=\"https://books.google.com/books?id=39ENDgAAQBAJ\" target=\"_blank\">According</a> to the Roman historian (and the emperor's contemporary) Cassius Dio, Elagabalus prostituted himself in brothels and taverns and was one of the first historical figures on record to be looking for sex reassignment surgery.</p><p>He was eventually murdered in 222 in an assassination plot engineered by his own grandmother Julia Maesa.</p><p><strong>5. Vitellius</strong></p><p>Emperor for just eight months, from April 19th to December 20th of the year 69 AD, Vitellius made some key administrative contributions to the empire but is ultimately remembered as a cruel glutton. He was<a href=\"https://www.perseus.tufts.edu/hopper/text?doc=Perseus%3Atext%3A1999.02.0132%3Alife%3Dvit.\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"> described</a> by Suetonius as overly fond of eating and drinking, to the point where he would eat at banquets four times a day while sending out the Roman navy to get him rare foods. He also had little social grace, inviting himself over to the houses of different noblemen to eat at their banquets, too.</p><p class=\"shortcode-media shortcode-media-rebelmouse-image\">\n        <img class=\"rm-shortcode\" data-rm-shortcode-id=\"cde66d6bb67ab4506a3f467de5f177bc\" data-rm-shortcode-name=\"rebelmouse-image\" id=\"09baf\" loading=\"lazy\" src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjQwMDM3NS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY2MjA4ODA2Mn0.5EgOSTbcGswHlZBGgTqSIFU7rk_yfQ6inRpSsgzXaxU/img.jpg?width=980\"/>\n        \n        \n        <small class=\"image-media media-caption\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Caption...\">Vitellius dragged through the streets of Rome.</small><small class=\"image-media media-photo-credit\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Credit...\">Credit:  Georges Rochegrosse. 1883.</small></p><p>He was also quite vicious and reportedly either had his own mother starved to death or approved a poison with which she committed suicide.<br/></p><p>Vitellius was ultimately murdered in brutal fashion by supporters of the rival emperor Vespasian, who dragged him through Rome's streets, then likely beheaded him and threw his body into the Tiber river. \"Yet I was once your emperor,\" were supposedly his last words, <a href=\"https://www.jstor.org/stable/20304642\" target=\"_blank\">wrote</a> historian Cassius Dio.</p><p><strong>6. Caracalla</strong></p><p>Marcus Aurelius Antoninus I ruled Rome from 211 to 217 AD on his own (while previously co-ruling with his father Septimius Severus from 198). \"Caracalla\"' was his nickname, referencing a hooded coat from Gaul that he brought into Roman fashion. </p><p>He started off his rise to individual power by murdering his younger brother Geta, who was named co-heir by their father. Caracalla's bloodthirsty tyranny didn't stop there. He wiped out Geta's supporters and was known to execute any opponents to his or Roman rule. For instance, he slaughtered up to 20,000 citizens of Alexandria after a local theatrical satire dared to mock him.</p><p class=\"shortcode-media shortcode-media-rebelmouse-image\">\n        <img class=\"rm-shortcode\" data-rm-shortcode-id=\"501dcb258ec363ff6839b2dfea4363e3\" data-rm-shortcode-name=\"rebelmouse-image\" id=\"22feb\" loading=\"lazy\" src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjQwMDQxNy9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY3MDczMzMwOH0.yChSajxg2It6LR5v_AIhJZ6If0mSRMVzG-aG_z8YEMI/img.jpg?width=980\"/>\n        \n        \n        <small class=\"image-media media-caption\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Caption...\">Geta Dying in His Mother's Arms.</small><small class=\"image-media media-photo-credit\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Credit...\">Credit: Jacques Pajou (1766-1828)</small></p><p>One of the positive outcomes of his rule was the Edict of Caracalla, which gave Roman citizenship to all free men in the empire. He was also known for building gigantic<a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Baths_of_Caracalla\" target=\"_blank\"> baths</a>.<br/></p><p>Like others on this list, Caracalla met a brutal end, being assassinated by army officers, including the Praetorian prefect Opellius Macrinus, who installed himself as the next emperor. </p><p><strong>7. Tiberius</strong></p><p>As the second emperor, Tiberius (ruling from 42 BC to 16 AD) is known for a number of accomplishments, especially his military exploits. He was one of the Roman Empire's most successful generals, conquering Pannonia, Dalmatia, Raetia, and parts of Germania. </p><p>He was also remembered by his contemporaries as a rather sullen, perverse, and angry man. In the chapter on his life from <em>The Lives of the Twelve Caesars</em> by the historian Suetonius, Tiberius is said to have been disliked from an early age for his personality by even his family. Suetonius<a href=\"https://www.gutenberg.org/files/6400/6400-h/6400-h.htm#link2H_4_0004\" target=\"_blank\"> wrote</a> that his mother Antonia often called him \"an abortion of a man, that had been only begun, but never finished, by nature.\"</p><p class=\"shortcode-media shortcode-media-rebelmouse-image\">\n        <img class=\"rm-shortcode\" data-rm-shortcode-id=\"d12dda67daab9bafb7c992de6f8c1d7a\" data-rm-shortcode-name=\"rebelmouse-image\" id=\"0a6e3\" loading=\"lazy\" src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjQwMDQ0NS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY3NDYwMTA3Mn0._cbT2MPbeKhScMhDTAknMDuFl68JVNyL_gDq23U_P3E/img.jpg?width=980\"/>\n        \n        \n        <small class=\"image-media media-caption\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Caption...\">\"Orgy of the Times of Tiberius on Capri\".</small><small class=\"image-media media-photo-credit\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Credit...\">Painting by Henryk Siemiradzki. 1881.</small></p><p>Suetonius also<a href=\"https://www.livius.org/sources/content/suetonius/suetonius-on-tiberius-sex-life/\" target=\"_blank\"> paints</a> a damning picture of Tiberius after he retreated from public life to the island of Capri. His years on the island would put Jeffrey Epstein to shame. A horrendous pedophile, Tiberius had a reputation for \"depravities that one can hardly bear to tell or be told, let alone believe,\" Suetonius<a href=\"https://www.livius.org/sources/content/suetonius/suetonius-on-tiberius-sex-life/\" target=\"_blank\"> wrote</a>, describing how \"in Capri's woods and groves he arranged a number of nooks of venery where boys and girls got up as Pans and nymphs solicited outside bowers and grottoes: people openly called this 'the old goat's garden,' punning on the island's name.\"<br/></p><p>There's much, much more — far too salacious and, frankly, disgusting to repeat here. For the intrepid or morbidly curious reader, here's a <a href=\"https://www.livius.org/sources/content/suetonius/suetonius-on-tiberius-sex-life/\" target=\"_blank\">link</a> for more information.</p><p>After he died, Tiberius was fittingly succeeded in emperorship by his grandnephew and adopted grandson Caligula.</p><img
